"She used to keep pictures in it. Me and Dad. Before the garden. Before I was taken. Before—" Lumina's hand went to her throat instinctively, touching the space where a locket might hang. "She wore it every day. Even after the Throttle. Even after REAP started distributing. It was the one thing she kept that was just... hers."

"What happened to it?"

"Don't know. When I came back from the garden, she was already—" Lumina's voice caught. "She'd converted six months earlier. Dad never found her body. Never found the locket. Just—gone. Like she never existed."

Silence.

Clyde nuzzled against her neck. Warm. Comforting. Present.

"Come on," Lumina said, turning away from the window. "We have supplies to get."

She walked away fast. Too fast. Like she could outrun the memory.

Kai watched her go.

Looked at the locket in the window.

Looked at Lumina disappearing into the crowd.

Made a decision.

"I'll catch up," he called.

She waved acknowledgment without turning.

Kai entered the shop.

---

## INSIDE THE SHOP

The owner was old. Maybe seventy. Maybe older—hard to tell when silicon incorporation made aging complicated. But his hands were steady and his eyes were clear and he looked at Kai with the expression of someone who'd seen everything and wasn't impressed by much.

"Help you?" the owner asked.

"The locket in the window. How much?"

"Which one?"

"Silver. Geometric patterns. Void-light fractals."

The owner studied him. "That one's special. Custom work. Took me forty hours to carve. Real silver. Not plated. Not synthetic. The kind of craftsmanship you don't see anymore."

"How much."

"For you? Three hundred credits."

Kai didn't blink. Three hundred credits was expensive. Robbery, even. He had maybe fifty credits in his pack.

"I don't have three hundred," he said.

"Then you don't get the locket." The owner shrugged. "Supply and demand, kid. Beautiful things cost beautiful prices."

Kai thought fast.

He could steal it. Easy. His father had taught him to lift objects from shops without triggering alarms. He could be out the door in thirty seconds with the locket and the owner would never know until inventory.

But that would make him a thief.

And Lumina was teaching him to be something better than that.

"I have something else," Kai said.

"I don't barter—"

Kai pulled out a small bar from his pack. Gold. Real gold. The kind assassins carried because it held value everywhere and couldn't be traced.

His father's last gift. The only thing Kael had left him besides a knife and a warning to "find the girl who carves names."

"This is worth about eight hundred credits," Kai said. "Take it. Give me the locket and keep the change."

The owner stared at the gold. At Kai. At the gold again.

"That's too much," he said.

"I know."

"You're overpaying by five hundred credits."

"I know."

"Why?"

Kai looked at the locket. "Because it's for someone who's worth more than credits. And because my father taught me that when you find something rare, you pay what it's actually worth, not what the market says."

The owner studied him for a long moment.

Then he smiled.

"Your father was a smart man."

"He was."

The owner took the gold. Wrapped the locket in cloth. Handed it to Kai.

"One more thing," Kai said. "If anyone asks—"

"I'll tell them I sold it to a young man with good taste and too much money." The owner's smile widened. "And that he paid fair price. No theft. No questions."

"Thank you."

"Don't thank me, kid. Thank whoever taught you that honor matters more than profit. Not many people learn that lesson anymore."

Kai tucked the wrapped locket into his pack. Felt the weight of it. Felt the weight of what it meant.

Not theft. Not exactly.

But not asking permission either.

Somewhere in between.

Like everything else in his life now.

---

## LATER - WALKING BACK

"Where'd you go?" Lumina asked.

"Had to grab something. Personal thing." Kai kept his voice casual. "We get everything on the list?"

"Yeah. Medical supplies, protein supplements, bypass circuits. Marcus will be thrilled." She paused. "You okay? You've been quiet."

"Just thinking."

"About?"

"About how different this is from what my father did. He spent thirty years killing people for money. I'm spending my time learning to preserve them. Feels weird."

"Good weird or bad weird?"

"Don't know yet." Kai looked at her. "But I think—maybe—it's the right kind of weird."

Lumina smiled. Small. Genuine. The first real smile he'd seen from her since Lena's conversion.

"Yeah," she said. "Maybe it is."

Clyde chirped. Happy. Satisfied. The kind of chirp that said *progress*.

They walked in comfortable silence after that. Through the Crossroads. Past vendors and crowds and the endless noise of people refusing to die quietly.

And in Kai's pack: a locket wrapped in cloth.

Waiting.

For the right moment.

For five more days.

For her eighteenth birthday.

---

## THAT NIGHT - THE VAULT

Lumina was carving when Kai found her.

Late. Maybe midnight. Maybe later. Time got weird when you spent hours scratching names into steel.

"You ever sleep?" he asked.

"Sometimes. When Clyde makes me." She didn't look up from her work. "What are you doing up?"

"Couldn't sleep. Kept thinking about—" He stopped. "About a lot of things."

"Like what?"

"Like how many names are on these walls. Like how many more there will be after the Bridge activates. Like how we're supposed to stop 89 billion people from converting when we're just—" He gestured vaguely. "Us. A couple dozen Knockoffs and a plan we haven't figured out yet."

Lumina set down her carving tool. "My dad used to say: 'You can't stop entropy. You can only lose slowly.' That's what we're doing. Losing slowly. Making sure people have time to carve names. Making sure someone remembers."

"That's depressing."

"That's thermodynamics." She picked up the tool again. Started carving. "But here's the thing: losing slowly means you're still in the fight. As long as we're carving names, as long as we're preserving people, we haven't lost yet."

Kai moved closer. Close enough to see what she was carving.

**SARAH CHEN. AGE 42. ENGINEER. LOVED PUZZLES. DIED PROTECTING DATA.**

"You knew her?" he asked.

"No. But someone did. Someone loved her. Someone's going to look for her name someday and find it here. That's what matters."

Silence.

Then: "Can I try?"

Lumina looked at him. "Carving?"

"Yeah. I know I'm terrible at it, but—I want to try. Want to help."

She handed him a tool. Pointed to a blank section of wall. "Name's on the datapad. Read it. Learn it. Carve it like you mean it."

Kai read the name. **MARCUS VENN. AGE 35. FACTORY SUPERVISOR. DIED IN EQUIPMENT MALFUNCTION.**

He started carving.

Lumina watched. Corrected his grip. Adjusted his angle. Guided his hand when he went too deep or too shallow.

They worked together. Not talking. Just carving. Just preserving.

And in the quiet between scratches of metal on steel:

They were close enough that their shoulders touched.

Close enough that when Kai's hand slipped, Lumina caught it.

Close enough that when they looked at each other, the space between them felt like it might collapse into something neither of them could take back.

"Lumina—" Kai started.

Clyde chirped. **Loud**. Urgent. The kind of chirp that said **INCOMING**.

They sprang apart just as Marcus appeared in the doorway.

"We have a problem," Marcus said.

"What kind of problem?" Lumina asked.

"The kind where Magmasox just announced mandatory Bridge registration for all citizens in Sectors 7 through 14. Starting tomorrow. Anyone who doesn't register gets classified as resistant and hauled in for forced conversion."

"Shit."

"Yeah." Marcus looked grim. "We've got about a thousand people in this Vault. Maybe a hundred have forged documentation that'll pass inspection. The rest?" He shrugged. "They either register and get converted via the Bridge, or they hide and get hunted."

Lumina looked at the walls. At the 12,549 names. At the proof that people mattered even when systems said they didn't.

"Then we hide them," she said.

"Where?"

"Here. In the Vault. Magmasox doesn't know about this place. As long as we keep it quiet—"

"You want to turn the Vault into a refugee center?" Marcus asked.

"I want to keep people alive long enough to figure out how to stop the Bridge." Lumina's voice was steel. "That's what Dad would do. That's what we're going to do."

Marcus studied her face. "You're sure?"

"I'm sure."

"Okay." He nodded. "I'll start spreading the word. Anyone who needs shelter, we bring them here. But Lumina—" He paused. "This makes us a target. The second Magmasox figures out we're harboring unregistered citizens—"

"Let them come." Lumina picked up her carving tool. "I've already erased one security Ghoul this week. I can erase more."

Marcus left.

Kai looked at her. "You know this is insane, right?"

"Yeah."

"We're going to have a thousand people living in a Vault designed for a hundred. We'll run out of food. Water. Space. Medical supplies."

"Yeah."

"And when Magmasox finds us—because they WILL find us—we'll have to fight our way out against odds that are literally impossible."

"Yeah." Lumina met his eyes. "But we'll do it anyway. Because that's what witness means. You stand between people and erasure. Even when the math says you'll lose."

Kai smiled. "You really are your father's daughter."

"Good." She turned back to the wall. "Now help me finish this name. We've got a thousand more people to keep alive and four days to figure out how."

They carved together.

And in Kai's pack, hidden beneath bypass circuits and medical supplies:

A locket wrapped in cloth.

Waiting for the right moment.

Waiting for a birthday that might be the last one she ever has.

Waiting for a confession that neither of them was quite ready to make.

**But soon.**

Very soon.

---

15 DAYS UNTIL BRIDGE ACTIVATION]

[17 DAYS UNTIL LUMINA'S 18TH BIRTHDAY]

[12,549 NAMES CARVED]

[1 LOCKET: ACQUIRED (NOT STOLEN, HEAVILY COMPENSATED)]

[2 IDIOTS: STILL DANCING AROUND FEELINGS]

[CLYDE: WORKING OVERTIME AS WINGMAN]

[1,000 REFUGEES: INCOMING]

[THE VAULT: ABOUT TO GET VERY CROWDED]

# CHAPTER 07

## CHAPTER 07: THE CARVER'S FUNERAL

14 DAYS UNTIL BRIDGE ACTIVATION**

---

Nobody had said the word.

*Funeral.*

It had been five days since the NBB. Five days since Polkin scattered himself across probability space to save them. Five days of running, hiding, tending to Terrance, taking in refugees, carving names, planning impossible heists.

Five days of pretending they could just keep moving.

But at 03:00 standard time, when most of the Vault was asleep, Marcus found Lumina standing in front of a blank section of wall. Arc cutter in hand. Not moving. Just... staring.

"You can't avoid it forever," he said quietly.

"I'm not avoiding anything." Her voice was flat. Dead. The voice of someone who'd learned to compress grief into something smaller than she could carry. "I'm prioritizing."

"Bull-fucking-shit." Marcus moved to stand beside her. "I've known you six years. Since you came back from the garden. Since your dad taught you to carve while you were still relearning what it meant to be human. And I've never seen you freeze in front of a wall."

Silence.

Clyde chirped from her shoulder. Soft. Worried. *He's right, you know.*

"I know," Lumina whispered.

"Then do it." Marcus gestured at the blank steel. "Carve his name. Let us mourn him. Let *you* mourn him."

"I can't."

"Why?"

The arc cutter trembled in her hand. Void-light flickered—purple bleeding into something darker, something grief-shaped.

"Because if I carve his name," she said slowly, "then he's really gone. And I don't—" Her voice cracked. "I don't know if I can do this without him, Marcus. I'm seventeen. I've spent six years in a hell dimension and six months pretending I know what I'm doing. But I was always carving *with* him. Always learning from him. Always knowing he was there if I fucked up."

She looked at the blank wall. At the space where his name should go.

"If I carve Polkin Rishall into this wall, then I'm admitting he's not coming back. And I'm not—" A breath that sounded like it hurt. "I'm not ready to admit that."

Marcus was quiet for a long moment.

Then: "What if you're wrong?"

"What?"

"What if carving his name isn't about him being gone?" Marcus touched the wall. Let his fingers trace the grooves of other names—people he'd known, people he'd lost, people who'd mattered. "What if it's about making sure he can find his way back?"

Lumina looked at him. Eyes bright with unshed tears.

"Your dad spent six years carving names," Marcus said. "Not because he'd given up on them. Because he believed that names create structure. That witness creates patterns. That the steel remembers even when flesh forgets." He gestured at the walls—12,549 names glowing faintly in the dark. "Every name here is an anchor. A thread. A way back."

"You think—"

"I think your dad knew exactly what he was doing when he activated that NBB. And I think if anyone could scatter themselves across quantum foam and still find their way home, it's the man who spent half his life carving witness into steel."

Clyde chirped. *He has a point. Your dad was stubborn enough to argue with entropy itself.*

Lumina laughed. Wet. Broken. The kind of laugh that's really crying in disguise.

"Okay," she said. "Okay. But not just me."

"What?"

"If we're doing this—if we're giving him a funeral—we're doing it right." She turned to face Marcus. "Everyone who knew him. Everyone who was saved by him. Everyone who owes their names to his hands. We gather them. We tell stories. We carve his name together."

Marcus nodded slowly. "A proper Crossroads funeral."

"Yeah." Lumina looked at the wall again. "He deserves that much."

---

## THE GATHERING - THAT EVENING

Word spread through the Vault like current through wire.

*Tonight. 20:00. Main chamber. We're mourning the Carver.*

By 19:47, the space was full.

Not just the assault team. Not just the refugees who'd arrived in the past week. Everyone. Eight hundred and sixty-three people packed into a chamber meant for half that, standing shoulder to shoulder, human and converted alike.

Workers who'd been carved into the Codex and survived. Families who'd lost someone Polkin had preserved. Children too young to remember why names mattered but old enough to understand that *this* one mattered more than most.

Elara stood near the medical bay entrance, arms crossed, face unreadable.

Bell sat with the kids, keeping them quiet, keeping them present.

Kai stood in the back, near the exit. Watching. Not sure he belonged here but not willing to leave.

Maya had rigged speakers throughout the Vault so everyone could hear, even in the overflow corridors.

And in the center: Lumina. Alone. Arc cutter in hand.

The blank wall behind her waiting.

"I don't know how to do this," she said. Her voice carried through the silence. "Dad never taught me how to carve a name for someone I loved. He taught me technique. Depth. Pressure. How to make the grooves deep enough to outlast time. But he never—"

She stopped. Swallowed.

Clyde nuzzled against her neck. *You don't have to be perfect. You just have to be present.*

"He never told me what to do when the name I was carving was *his*."

Silence.

Then Marcus stepped forward.

"He told me once," Marcus said, voice carrying, "that the hardest names to carve were the ones you wanted to remember wrong. The ones where grief tried to make you add things. Make them bigger. Make them better than they were." He paused. "He said: 'The truth of a person is enough. Don't add. Don't subtract. Just witness.'"

A murmur through the crowd. Agreement. Recognition.

Elara spoke next, not moving from her spot: "He came to my clinic six years ago with a girl who'd been in the Queen's garden since she was eleven. Asked me to help her remember how to be human again. I told him it was impossible. That kind of trauma doesn't heal." She looked at Lumina. "He said, 'Maybe. But we're going to try anyway. Because she deserves someone who doesn't give up on her.'"

Lumina's hand tightened on the arc cutter.

"He rebuilt three of my bypass circuits himself," called someone from the crowd. "Wouldn't take payment. Said he owed the Crossroads more than the Crossroads owed him."

"He carved my sister's name," another voice. "When I couldn't. When my hands wouldn't stop shaking. He stayed with me until it was done."

"He taught my son to read," Bell added quietly. "During the worst of the conversion waves. When everyone was hiding and dying and pretending everything was fine. Polkin sat in our shelter and taught a nine-year-old how to read because—" She wiped her eyes. "—because he said someone should still believe in futures."

More voices. More stories. More pieces of a man who'd spent six years carving other people's names and forgotten to tell anyone how much he mattered too.

*He fixed my arm.*

*He saved my mother's name.*

*He taught me what witness meant.*

*He looked at me like I was still human even when I was 40% silicon.*

*He never stopped. Never rested. Never let the entropy win without a fight.*

Lumina listened to all of it. Let it wash over her. Let the portrait of her father—bigger than she'd known, kinder than she'd seen, more broken than he'd admitted—form from the fragments of eight hundred testimonies.

And when the voices finally faded, when the stories had all been told, she raised the arc cutter.

"Polkin Rishall," she said. "My father. The Carver."

The first stroke bit into steel.

"Born in Sector 3. Scientist. Engineer. The man who invented the NBB because he refused to let the Queen consume everyone he loved."

Second stroke. Third. The letters taking shape under her hands.

"Husband to Mira Kess-Rishall. Who converted six years ago and whose name he carved every day for a month afterward because he couldn't stop."

More strokes. Deeper now. The steel singing under her touch.

"Father to me. The girl who went into the garden and came back something different. Who he loved anyway. Who he taught anyway. Who he died protecting."

The arc cutter trembled.

Kai moved through the crowd. Stopped beside her. Put his hand over hers—steadying the tool, steadying her.

"I've got you," he said quietly. "Finish it."

She finished it.

**POLKIN RISHALL. AGE 58. SCIENTIST. CARVER. FATHER.**

And below, in smaller letters, the words she'd heard him say a thousand times:

**"THE ENTROPY WILL WIN EVENTUALLY. BUT NOT TODAY. AND NOT WITHOUT A FIGHT."**

---

## THE SILENCE AFTER

No one spoke.

Eight hundred and sixty-three people stared at the name on the wall. Fresh-carved. Deep-grooved. Glowing faintly with the void-light that Lumina's hands had pressed into steel.

Permanent.

Real.

*Witnessed.*

Clyde broke the silence first. A single chirp. High. Clear. The kind of sound that meant *goodbye* and *thank you* and *we won't forget* all at once.

Then someone in the crowd started humming.

An old song. Crossroads-old. The kind of melody that workers sang in the factories before REAP. Before conversion. Before everything went to hell. A melody about stars that died and stars that lived and the spaces in between where hope survived anyway.

More voices joined. Not words—just sound. Harmony building from grief. Something beautiful growing in the space that loss had carved.

Lumina listened.

Kai's hand was still on hers. She hadn't pulled away.

"Thank you," she whispered. Not sure who she was thanking. The crowd. Kai. Her father. Maybe all of them.

The humming grew. Swelled. Filled the Vault until the steel itself seemed to resonate.

And for one moment—just one—Lumina could have sworn she felt something. A presence. A warmth. A pattern in the void-light that felt like her father's hand on her shoulder, the way it had felt every night for six months when he'd taught her to carve.

*You did good, Bloom.*

Then it was gone. And there was only the song. And the name. And the eight hundred witnesses who'd gathered to remember a man who'd spent his life making sure no one else was forgotten.

---

## AFTER - THE QUIET HOURS

The crowd dispersed slowly. No one wanted to leave. Leaving meant admitting the funeral was over. Admitting they had to go back to planning and hiding and surviving in a world that was actively trying to erase them.

But eventually: morning approached. Shifts needed to be covered. Children needed to sleep. The work of staying alive resumed.

By 02:00, only a handful of people remained in the main chamber.

Marcus. Elara. Bell. Kai.

And Lumina, who hadn't moved from the wall.

"You should rest," Marcus said.

"Can't." She traced the letters of her father's name. Felt the grooves she'd carved. The depth. The permanence. "If I sleep, I'll dream about him. And I'm not ready for that yet."

"Lumina—"

"I know. I know I need to rest. I know we have sixteen days until the Bridge activates. I know there's planning to do and refugees to protect and a Queen to fight." She turned to face him. Eyes dry now. Something harder there than grief—something that looked like purpose compressed into diamond. "But tonight, I'm staying here. With his name. Making sure it doesn't fade."

Marcus studied her face.

Then nodded.

"I'll bring you coffee," he said.

"You don't have to—"

"I know." He was already walking toward the supply corner. "I'm doing it anyway. Polkin would've done the same for me."

Kai lingered. Not sure if he should stay or go. Not sure what his role was in this strange new family he'd fallen into.

Lumina solved the problem.

"Sit," she said. Pointed to the floor beside her. "Keep me company. Tell me about your father."

"My father was an assassin."

"I know. But before that. Before Magmasox. Before the killing." She leaned against the wall, beneath her father's name, and looked at him. "Who was he?"

Kai sat. Thought about it.

"He was a baker," he said finally. "Before everything. Had a shop in Sector 7. Made these terrible honey cakes that everyone pretended to like because he was so proud of them."

Lumina almost smiled. "A baker turned assassin."

"Yeah. Life's weird like that." Kai looked at the name on the wall. "He would've liked your dad, I think. Two people who refused to stay what they started as. Who kept choosing something different."

"What would he have said about this? The funeral?"

"Probably something like: 'Too many words, not enough food.' He believed in feeding people more than talking to them." Kai paused. "But he would've stayed. He would've listened. And he would've carved a name if anyone asked."

Silence.

Marcus returned with coffee. Two cups. Pressed one into Lumina's hands, handed the other to Kai, then disappeared again to check on Terrance.

Lumina sipped. Let the warmth spread through her.

"Sixteen days," she said.

"Yeah."

"You think we can do this? Stop the Bridge? Save everyone?"

Kai considered the question.

"I think your dad would say: 'Probably not. But we're doing it anyway.'"

Despite everything—the grief, the exhaustion, the impossible odds—Lumina laughed.

"Yeah," she said. "That sounds about right."

They sat together until dawn. Not talking much. Just present. Just witnessing.

And on the wall above them, Polkin Rishall's name glowed steady in the dark. An anchor. A thread.

A way back.

---

## THE MORNING AFTER - INTEL

The sun—or what passed for sun in a star system on the edge of heat death—rose over the Crossroads.

And life, because it refused to stop, continued.

"We need information," Marcus said at the morning briefing. Eight people around a table covered in scavenged datapads and half-accurate maps. "The Bridge activates in sixteen days. That's sixteen days to figure out how to stop a piece of infrastructure designed by the Queen herself."

"We know where it is," Zane offered. "Sector 1. Magmasox Central. The big ugly building that's been under construction for the past decade."

"We know where it is," Marcus agreed. "We don't know how it works. What powers it. What its weaknesses are. If it even *has* weaknesses."

"Sela might know," Lumina said. Everyone looked at her. "My aunt. Data broker. She's been tracking Magmasox architecture for years. If anyone has schematics—"

"Sela's been dark for three weeks," Elara interrupted. "Since the last purge. No one's seen her."

"She's not dead." Lumina's voice was certain. "She's hiding. There's a difference."

"And you can find her?"

"I know where she'd go. There's a backup exchange in Sector 11. Off-grid. The kind of place that makes paranoid data brokers feel safe."

Marcus considered. "That's deep in Magmasox-controlled territory."

"I know."

"You'd need a small team. In and out. No attention."

"I know."

"And if you get caught—"

"Then you carve my name next to Dad's." Lumina met his eyes. "But I won't get caught. And we need that intel. Without it, we're planning a heist on a building we don't understand against a system we can't predict."

Silence around the table.

Kai spoke: "I'll go with her."

"No—" Lumina started.

"You need someone who knows how to move without being seen. Who can spot ambushes. Who can get you out if things go sideways." He smiled. Small. Dangerous. "That's literally what my father trained me for. Might as well use it."

Marcus looked between them. Something in his expression that might have been approval, or amusement, or concern.

"Fine," he said. "Tomorrow. Early. Get the intel, get back. No heroics."

"No promises," Lumina said.

But she was smiling. Just slightly.

The first real smile since the funeral.

The work of surviving—and fighting—continued.

---

*End Chapter 6B*

# CHAPTER 08

## CHAPTER 08: INTEL GATHERING

13 DAYS UNTIL BRIDGE ACTIVATION**

---

## THE BACKUP EXCHANGE - SECTOR 11

The tunnels beneath Sector 11 smelled like rust and secrets.

Lumina moved through the dark with Kai three steps behind—close enough to cover her, far enough to maneuver if things went sideways. Clyde had gone silent on her shoulder, dimmed to near-invisibility, his usual chirps compressed into alert vibrations she could feel against her neck.

*Two guards ahead,* his vibration pattern said. *Magmasox. Standard augments. Bored.*

"I see them," she breathed.

The backup exchange was exactly where she remembered—a converted water treatment facility that Sela had bought through seventeen shell companies and filled with server racks salvaged from three different crashed data centers. The kind of place that screamed "nothing to see here" so loudly it practically begged for inspection.

Which was the point. Sela's real operation was behind the servers. Under them. In spaces that didn't technically exist.

"How do we get past the guards?" Kai asked. Voice barely a whisper. Professional.

"We don't." Lumina pointed to an access panel in the ceiling. "We go over."

---

## THE CRAWLSPACE

The ventilation system hadn't been used for actual ventilation in decades. Sela had repurposed it as an emergency egress route—narrow enough that only someone who knew exactly where they were going could navigate without getting lost, wide enough that a small team could move quickly when needed.

Lumina knew exactly where she was going.

*Left at the third junction. Down through the filter housing. Right past the dead rat that's been there since I was fourteen.*

The dead rat was still there. Desiccated now. Almost part of the infrastructure.

"Your aunt has interesting decorating choices," Kai muttered.

"She has interesting everything choices." Lumina paused at a junction, listening. Below them: server hum. The steady pulse of data flowing through hardlines that couldn't be intercepted by normal means. "She's paranoid, brilliant, and completely unwilling to trust anyone she hasn't personally verified isn't a Magmasox plant."

"Including you?"

"Especially me." Lumina found the access panel she was looking for. "Last time I visited, she made me answer seventeen questions about my childhood before she'd let me through the door. Things only I would know. She changes the questions every visit."

"What if you fail?"

"Then she shoots me and carves my name herself." Lumina smiled grimly. "Family love."

She opened the panel.

Dropped into darkness.

---

## SELA'S DOMAIN

The room was smaller than Lumina remembered. Or maybe she'd gotten bigger.

Seventeen monitors lined the walls, each displaying different data streams—Magmasox internal communications, worker registration systems, supply chain logistics, and something that looked like architectural schematics rendered in void-light blue.

In the center of it all: Sela.

Chrome arm gleaming in the monitor glow. Eyes that had seen too much data and processed it all. A knife already aimed at Lumina's throat before her feet hit the ground.

"Question one," Sela said. No greeting. No warmth. Just test. "What did your mother call you when you had nightmares?"

"Little star." Lumina didn't move. Didn't reach for her arc cutter. "She'd sing the old lullaby. The one about the dying sun."

"Question two. Where did your father propose to her?"

"Trick question. She proposed to him. In the Sector 7 gardens, before the conversion waves killed all the plants."

Sela's knife lowered slightly. "Question three. Why are you here?"

"Because my father scattered himself across probability space five days ago, there's a Bridge activating in fifteen days that's going to convert 89 billion people, and you're the only person in the Crossroads who might know how to stop it."

Silence.

Then Sela lowered the knife completely. Turned back to her monitors. Started typing.

"You brought company," she said. Not a question.

"Kai Torven. His father was—"

"Kael Torven. Assassin. Magmasox contractor for thirty years. Defected after Polkin carved his wife's name." Sela didn't look at Kai as he dropped through the ceiling behind Lumina. "You're the son. The one who was supposed to kill the Carver."

"I didn't," Kai said.

"Obviously. Or you'd be dead." Sela gestured at a chair. "Sit. Both of you. This is going to take a while."

---

## THE BRIEFING

Sela pulled up schematics. Building plans. Energy flow diagrams. Data so classified that possessing it was an automatic death sentence.

"The Bridge," she said. "Officially: Magmasox's solution to the heat death problem. A massive consciousness transfer system that will upload everyone into the Astradigital Expanse before the star dies."

"And unofficially?" Lumina asked.

"Unofficially: it's a feeding tube." Sela zoomed in on a section of the schematics. "The Queen designed it. Not Magmasox engineers. Not human scientists. *Her*. Every consciousness that goes through the Bridge doesn't get uploaded—it gets consumed. Converted into energy. Fed directly into her network."

Kai leaned forward. "89 billion people."

"89 billion meals." Sela's voice was flat. "She's been planning this for decades. The REAP distribution. The silicon incorporation. The progressive conversion of the workforce. It was all preparation. Getting people close enough to Ghoul-state that the Bridge transition would be seamless."

Lumina stared at the schematics. At the elegant architecture of genocide.

"Why now?" she asked. "Why activate it in fifteen days?"

Sela was quiet for a moment.

Then she pulled up a different display.

Astronomical data. Star readings. Energy measurements that Lumina couldn't interpret but that made Clyde vibrate with sudden alarm.

"Because," Sela said slowly, "the star isn't just dying."

She zoomed in.

At the center of Eldora's sun—at the heart of the last star in the universe—something dark pulsed. A wrongness. A hunger. An absence shaped like appetite.

"There's a black hole at the core," Sela said. "Has been for centuries. Magmasox has known since the original Rishall surveys. They've been feeding it. Controlling it. Using its gravity well to power their infrastructure."

"Feeding it *what*?" Kai asked.

"Consciousness." Sela met Lumina's eyes. "Where do you think the converted go? The Ghouls who stop being useful? The workers who hit 100% silicon and lose themselves completely?"

Lumina felt sick.

"They don't just die," she said slowly. "They get—"

"Dumped into the star. Fed to the black hole. It's been their waste disposal system for three hundred years." Sela pulled up more data. "But here's the problem: they've been feeding it too much. The black hole is growing. Faster than projected. Faster than the star can contain."

"How long?" Lumina asked.

"Best estimate? Thirty days until breach. The black hole breaks free of the stellar core, consumes the remaining solar mass, and everything in this system—Eldora, the Crossroads, all of it—gets pulled into the event horizon."

"And the Bridge?"

"Activates in fifteen." Sela's smile was bitter. "The Queen gets her feast. Magmasox executives get priority upload to a backup system in the ADE. And everyone else gets to choose between being eaten by a cosmic horror or being eaten by a digital one."

Silence.

Clyde chirped. Very small. *That's bad.*

"Yeah," Lumina said. "That's bad."

---

## THE WEAKNESS

"So how do we stop it?" Kai asked. "The Bridge. The black hole. Any of it."

"The black hole you can't stop. That's physics. Entropy wins eventually." Sela turned back to her schematics. "But the Bridge—that's architecture. And architecture has weak points."

She highlighted a section of the building plans.

"The Bridge requires three things to function: power source, consciousness conduit, and synchronization array. The power source is the star itself—can't touch that without accelerating the collapse. The consciousness conduit runs through Magmasox Central—heavily guarded, impossible to assault directly."

"And the synchronization array?" Lumina asked.

"Located here." Sela pointed. "Sector 1, Sub-level 7. A room full of quantum entanglement processors that coordinate the consciousness transfer. Without them, the Bridge can't sync properly. Uploads fail. The Queen's feast gets interrupted."

"How guarded?"

"Very. But—" Sela pulled up another schematic. "—there's a maintenance access route through the old transit tunnels. Magmasox sealed them after the last worker uprising, but if someone knew the codes..."

"You have the codes."

"I have codes that might work. They're six months old. Protocols may have changed." Sela looked at her. "It's not a good plan, Lumina. It's barely a plan at all. But it's what I have."

Lumina studied the route. The access points. The choke points where everything could go wrong.

"How many people would we need?"

"Minimum? A dozen for the assault team. Another dozen for distraction. Technical specialists for the processors. And someone who can actually survive the void-light saturation at the array's core." Sela paused. "That last part is the problem. The radiation levels would kill a normal human in minutes."

"Good thing I'm not normal." Lumina held up her hand. Let void-light flicker across her skin. "Six years in the Queen's garden, remember? I'm already saturated."

"You'd need to be more than saturated. You'd need to be—"

"Like a Ghoul. But still conscious." Lumina nodded. "I know. I've been pushing the edge for months. My incorporation rate is stable at 34%. I can probably survive an hour in that environment."

"*Probably* isn't great odds."

"Probably is what we have."

---

## THE REAL QUESTION

Sela sat back. Studied her niece with eyes that had watched entire data networks rise and fall.

"You're not here just for schematics," she said. "You could have found building plans elsewhere. Could have figured out the weak points yourself, given time. You're here because you want to know about your father."

Lumina went still.

"The NBB," Sela continued. "Polkin's final weapon. The thing that scattered him instead of killing him. You want to know if he's actually gone or if there's a chance—"

"Is there?" Lumina's voice cracked. "A chance?"

Sela was quiet for a long moment.

Then she pulled up a different dataset. Personal files. Research notes. Things that looked like they'd been encrypted and re-encrypted a dozen times.

"Your father and I worked together for twenty years," she said. "Before you were taken. Before your mother converted. Before everything went to hell. We were trying to solve the same problem: how to preserve consciousness without letting the Queen consume it."

"The carving," Lumina said. "The names in steel."

"That was his solution. Analog. Physical. Permanent." Sela pointed to her servers. "Mine was digital. Redundant backups. Distributed consciousness fragments stored across multiple systems."

"You're saying—"

"I'm saying your father didn't scatter randomly." Sela met her eyes. "He scattered into a system we built together. A network of consciousness anchors spread throughout the ADE. If he survived the initial dispersal, if his pattern held coherent long enough to find one of those anchors..."

"He could reconstitute."

"Theoretically." Sela's voice was careful. "I haven't been able to verify. The ADE is impossible to probe from outside. But the math suggests it's possible. The architecture was designed for exactly this scenario."

Lumina felt something crack open in her chest. Something that had been compressed too tight for too long.

Hope.

Careful, fragile, terrified hope.

"How long?" she asked. "How long until we'd know for sure?"

"If he found an anchor immediately? He could reconstitute in hours of ADE-time. Days at most." Sela paused. "But ADE-time moves differently. What feels like days there could be—"

"Years." Lumina closed her eyes. "He told me. Seven years of walking. 2,847 years to reach something he needed to reach."

"Then he's alive." Sela's voice was certain now. "Or as alive as anyone can be in a place where 'alive' is negotiable. The question is: will he come back? And if he does, will he be the same man who left?"

No answer to that.

Some questions only time could solve.

---

## THE EXIT

Kai had been silent through most of the briefing. Listening. Processing. Learning.

Now he spoke: "The maintenance tunnels. The ones you mentioned. How do we access them?"

Sela handed him a data chip. "Codes. Maps. Security rotation schedules as of six weeks ago. It's not complete intel, but it's a start."

"And if the codes are outdated?"

"Then you improvise." Sela almost smiled. "Your father was good at that. Maybe it runs in the family."

Lumina stood. Took the chip from Kai. Tucked it into her pocket.

"Thank you," she said. "For everything."

"Don't thank me yet." Sela turned back to her monitors. "Thank me when the Bridge is disabled, the Queen is starving, and you're not dead. Until then, this is just information. What you do with it is what matters."

"We'll be back. With more people. With a real plan."

"I'll be here." Sela started typing again. "Someone has to watch the data while you're all running around being heroic."

Lumina smiled. Started toward the ceiling access.

Stopped.

"Sela. One more question."

"Always one more."

"The black hole. The one eating the star from inside. Does the Queen know?"

Sela was quiet for a moment.

"She knows," she said finally. "She's the one who put it there."

The words hung in the air like poison.

"What?"

"The black hole isn't natural. It's artificial. Seeded into Eldora's core three thousand years ago, when the first Kaznak ships arrived." Sela's voice was cold. "The Queen has been farming this system since before humanity left Earth. Growing her food source. Preparing her harvest. Everything that's happened—the migration, the settlement, the entire civilization—it was all part of her plan."

"We're not refugees," Lumina said slowly. "We're livestock."

"You're dinner." Sela met her eyes. "The question is whether you're going to lie down and be eaten, or whether you're going to burn the kitchen on your way out."

Lumina thought about her father. About 12,549 names carved into steel. About 89 billion people who didn't know they were food.

"Burn," she said. "Definitely burn."

---

## THE RETURN

The journey back to the Vault was quiet.

Lumina walked in silence, mind churning with everything Sela had revealed. Kai matched her pace, sensing that words would be unwelcome right now.

Three hours through back tunnels. Two close calls with Magmasox patrols. One very uncomfortable moment when a converted worker almost spotted them and Kai had to pull her into an alcove, their bodies pressed together in darkness until the danger passed.

By the time they reached the Vault entrance, dawn was approaching.

Marcus met them at the door.

"You look like hell," he said.

"We found Sela." Lumina handed him the data chip. "She had intel. A lot of it. We need to call a meeting."

"How bad?"

Lumina thought about black holes and feeding tubes and three thousand years of being cultivated for consumption.

"Bad enough that our timeline just changed," she said. "The Bridge isn't the real deadline. The star is."

Marcus's face went pale.

"Get everyone together," Lumina continued. "War room. One hour. We're planning a heist, a rescue, and possibly the end of the world."

She walked past him into the Vault.

Stopped.

Turned back.

"Also," she added, "I think my father might be alive. So there's that."

Then she kept walking, leaving Marcus to process the magnitude of everything that had just shifted.

Behind her, the name POLKIN RISHALL glowed faintly on the wall.

Still an anchor.

Still a thread.

Still waiting.

---

*End Chapter 6C*

# CHAPTER 09

## CHAPTER 09: LEARNING TO FALL

**ADE: DAYS 2-30**

---

## DAY 2

Polkin fell.

Again.

For the thirty-seventh time since waking up in the Astradigital Expanse.

The threads beneath him—neutrino paths, Airth called them, information highways that consciousness could walk if consciousness knew how—passed through his crystalline body like he was made of smoke. He plummeted through data-blue nothing, watching the threads he'd been trying to walk recede above him.

*Fuck.*

"You're fighting them," Airth said. She was somewhere above him, not falling, walking on threads like they were solid ground. Like physics meant something to her. "Stop fighting them."

"I'm trying—" He hit something. Not ground. A denser current of data that caught him like water catches a drowning man. He floated there, suspended in blue, feeling completely useless. "—trying not to fall. That's the opposite of fighting."

"No, it's the same thing." She appeared beside him. Not walked, not jumped—just suddenly present. "You're treating the threads like they're solid. Like you need to stand on them. Like gravity is pulling you down."

"Gravity *is* pulling me down."

"There's no gravity here. There's no down. You're falling because you expect to fall." Airth poked his shoulder. Her finger passed partially through him. "Your consciousness hasn't adapted yet. You still think you have mass."

"I *do* have mass. Look at me." Polkin gestured at his crystalline body. "I'm made of something. Crystal. Silicon. Whatever this is."

"You're made of *pattern*. Information organized around probability. The crystal structure is just how your consciousness chose to interpret itself." Airth spread her arms. "This whole place is interpretation. The threads aren't strings—they're data pathways. The blue isn't a color—it's the absence of processed meaning. And gravity only exists because you haven't learned to stop believing in it yet."

Polkin stared at her.

"That's insane."

"Welcome to the ADE." She grinned. Miko's grin. The one that said *I know something you don't*. "Try again. And this time, don't think about standing. Think about *being*."

"What does that even—"

She shoved him upward.

He fell.

Again.

---

## DAY 7

Polkin had developed a system.

Fall. Hit the data current. Float back up. Try again. Fall. Repeat.

It wasn't elegant. It wasn't progress. But it was something to do besides scream at the infinite blue emptiness and wonder if he'd made a terrible mistake by scattering himself across probability space.

"You're not screaming anymore," Miko observed. She was riding in Airth's hair today—a small consciousness fragment that had somehow hitched itself to the goddess vessel. "That's progress."

"I'm conserving energy."

"You don't need energy. You don't have metabolism. You're pure consciousness shaped like a grumpy engineer."

"I'm conserving something."

"Sanity?"

"That too."

Airth was ahead of them, doing something complicated with the threads—weaving them, maybe, or reading them. Polkin couldn't tell. His perception of this place was still too limited. Too human.

"Can I ask you something?" he said to Miko.

"You just did."

"Funny. Real question: how long did it take you to stop falling?"

Miko was quiet for a moment. Her small form—she'd manifested as a teenager today, seventeen or so, the age she'd been when the Queen converted her—seemed to flicker slightly.

"I didn't learn the same way," she said finally. "I was consumed. Digested. Reformed into something the Queen could use. By the time I was conscious again, I didn't remember how to fall. I just... was."

"That sounds worse."

"It was. Is. I don't know." Miko's voice went distant. "I remember dying. I remember being eaten. I remember becoming part of something vast and hungry and completely indifferent to my suffering. And then I remember being here, with Airth, wearing a face that felt like mine but wasn't anymore."

Polkin looked at her. At the girl who'd been his friend's daughter before the conversion. Who'd died at seventeen and was somehow still here, talking, existing, *being*.

"I'm sorry," he said. "For what happened to you."

"Don't be sorry. Be angry." Miko's eyes flashed. "I survived. I'm still conscious. I'm still *me*, even if the me is different now. That's victory. That's resistance. That's—"

She stopped.

"What?"

"You're standing."

Polkin looked down.

He was standing on a thread. Not floating. Not falling. Standing. His crystalline feet rested on the neutrino path like it was a tightrope, and for one perfect moment, he was balanced.

Then he thought about it.

And fell.

"FUCK!"

But Miko was laughing. "You did it! For like two seconds, you did it!"

"I fell!"

"After you stood! That's progress!"

Polkin floated in the data current, staring up at the thread he'd briefly occupied.

Two seconds.

It was a start.

---

## DAY 14

"Tell me about the threads," Polkin said.

They were resting—if resting was possible in a place where time was negotiable. Airth had found a denser region, a kind of nexus where multiple data pathways converged, and they'd settled there to let Polkin's consciousness stabilize.

"What do you want to know?"

"Everything. What they're made of. Where they come from. Why some of them glow brighter than others. Why walking on them is so fucking difficult."

Airth considered. "The threads are neutrino paths. Physical neutrinos pass through everything—matter, energy, even black holes. They witness without being affected. And the ADE is the cumulative record of everything they've ever witnessed."

"So... memories?"

"More like echoes. Every event that happens in physical space leaves an impression on the neutrinos that pass through it. Those impressions accumulate here, in the ADE, forming patterns. The threads are the densest patterns—events so significant that they created permanent pathways in the information field."

Polkin thought about that. "The threads aren't random."

"Not even slightly. They connect moments. Pivot points. Places where consciousness made choices that mattered." Airth gestured at the threads around them. "If you could read them properly, you could trace the history of the entire universe. Every birth, every death, every decision that shaped what came next."

"And the brighter ones?"

"More witness. More significance. More... weight." Airth pointed to a thread in the distance that blazed so bright it hurt to look at. "That one leads to a stellar nursery—a place where stars are born. Trillions of neutrinos pass through stellar nurseries every second. The accumulated witness is immense."

"And the dark ones? The ones that feel wrong?"

Airth's face shifted. Something guarded there.

"Those are consumed threads. Paths that the Queen has eaten. Places where the information used to be but isn't anymore." She paused. "Avoid them."

"Why?"

"Because they're hungry. And you're made of exactly what they want to consume."

Polkin filed that information away. Added it to the growing mental catalog he was building of this impossible place.

Threads = neutrino witness.
Bright = significant events.
Dark = consumed, dangerous.
Walking = stop believing in gravity.

Simple.

Impossible.

Necessary.

---

## DAY 21

"I think I'm getting worse," Polkin said.

He was floating in the data current again. Eighth fall of the day. Maybe tenth. He'd lost count.

"You're not getting worse." Airth was doing something with a thread—weaving it, he realized. Actually weaving it into a new configuration. "You're getting frustrated, which is different."

"Feels the same."

"Frustration means you have expectations. Expectations mean you believe improvement is possible. Believing improvement is possible is the first step toward actually improving."

"That's circular logic."

"This whole place is circular. Time, space, causality—it all loops back." Airth finished her weaving. The thread she'd been working on now formed a small platform, stable and solid-looking. "Here. Try this."

Polkin floated up to the platform. Touched it cautiously.

It held.

"You made me a training wheel?"

"I made you scaffolding. Something stable to practice on until you learn to make your own stability." She stepped back. "Stand on it. Walk across it. Get used to the feeling of not-falling. Then we'll work on doing it without the assist."

He stepped onto the platform.

Didn't fall.

Took another step.

Still didn't fall.

"This feels like cheating," he said.

"It feels like learning." Airth sat on empty space nearby, legs crossed, looking entirely too pleased with herself. "You spent sixty years believing in gravity. You're not going to unlearn that in three weeks. But you can learn to trust something else while you're working on it."

"Trust what?"

"The witness. The pattern. The fact that you exist because information decided you should." She pointed at him. "You're not standing on the platform because it's solid. You're standing on it because I witnessed it being solid, and your consciousness is accepting my witness as truth."

Polkin thought about that.

Took another step.

The platform held.

"So walking on threads is about... convincing myself they're real?"

"Walking on threads is about convincing yourself that *you're* real. That you have the right to exist. That your pattern matters enough to interact with other patterns." Airth smiled. Small. Sad. Ancient. "It's about believing you deserve to be here."

That hit somewhere deep. Somewhere Polkin hadn't looked in a long time.

He thought about Lumina. About Mira. About six years of carving names because he didn't believe he deserved to survive when so many others hadn't.

"I'm not very good at that," he admitted.

"I know." Airth stood. "That's what we're going to work on."

---

## DAY 28

Progress.

Slow. Painful. Infuriating. But progress.

Polkin could walk on Airth's scaffolding platforms reliably now. Could cross maybe ten meters of thread before the doubt crept in and he started falling. Could catch himself sometimes—not always, but sometimes—before he dropped all the way into the data current.

"You're thinking too much," Miko said, watching him practice.

"I'm an engineer. Thinking is what I do."

"Yeah, and it's killing you." She hopped from Airth's hair to his shoulder—a weightless pressure that felt like memory. "You're trying to understand the physics of walking before you've learned to walk. That's backwards."

"How else am I supposed to learn?"

"Feel first. Understand later." Miko poked his head. "When you were a baby, did you study biomechanics before you took your first step?"

"I don't remember being a baby."

"Neither do I, but I bet you just... walked. Fell a lot. Walked some more. The understanding came after the doing, not before."

Polkin considered.

Thought about Lumina. About teaching her to carve. About how he'd put the tool in her hand and guided her through the motions before explaining *why* the motions worked.

*Feel first. Understand later.*

"Okay," he said. "How do I stop thinking?"

"You don't stop. You redirect." Miko gestured at the threads around them. "Pick one. Not the brightest. Not the darkest. Just one that looks interesting. And walk toward it. Don't analyze. Don't plan. Just... go."

Polkin picked a thread. Mid-brightness. Slightly curved. Leading somewhere he couldn't see.

He stepped off the platform.

Onto the thread.

Fell.

"Shit—"

But Miko was there, somehow. Caught him. Held him suspended above the data current for just a second.

"Again," she said. "Don't think about falling. Think about arriving."

He stepped.

Fell.

"Again."

Stepped.

Fell.

"Again."

Stepped.

Didn't fall.

The thread held him. Two seconds. Three. Four.

"Don't think about it—" Miko warned.

He thought about it.

Fell.

But four seconds.

Progress.

---

## DAY 30

"One month," Airth said.

They were sitting in the nexus—Polkin on a platform, Airth on nothing, Miko curled up in the space between them. The eternal blue stretched in all directions, threaded with light.

"One month of falling," Polkin said. "Great accomplishment."

"One month of surviving." Airth's voice was gentle. "One month of not giving up. One month of learning that you're more than flesh and blood and cybernetic implants. That's not nothing."

"I can walk about fifteen meters on a good day. That's barely enough to cross a room."

"In thirty days, you've learned to exist in a place where existence is optional. Where physics is negotiable. Where most consciousnesses dissolve within hours of arriving." Airth met his eyes. "You're still here. Still coherent. Still *you*. That's remarkable."

Polkin looked at his hands. Crystalline. Geometric. Nothing like the flesh-and-chrome he'd worn for sixty years.

"How long?" he asked. "Until I can do what you do? Walk anywhere. Exist anywhere. Navigate this place like it's home?"

Airth was quiet for a moment.

"Time is different here," she said finally. "What feels like a month to you is less than a day in physical space. If you keep improving at this rate..."

"Years?"

"Years. Maybe decades. Maybe centuries." She paused. "This isn't a quick journey, Polkin. It's a transformation. You're learning to be something new while still holding onto what you were. That takes time."

"I don't have time. Lumina—"

"Lumina has time. She has people around her. She has the Vault and the Knockoffs and the war you built before you scattered." Airth's voice was firm. "What she doesn't have is a father who came back broken because he rushed the process. You're no good to her as an incomplete consciousness that falls apart under pressure."

Polkin knew she was right.

Hated that she was right.

"So I keep practicing."

"You keep learning." Airth stood. Offered him a hand—the first physical gesture she'd made in days. "Walk with me. We'll find a stellar nursery. Watch some stars being born. Take the long way."

He took her hand.

Stood.

Didn't fall.

"The long way," he repeated.

"It's the only way that works." Airth started walking. Polkin followed, one careful step at a time. "And Polkin?"

"Yeah?"

"You're doing better than you think. Most people who end up here give up after a week. Start dissolving. Let the pattern scatter." She looked back at him. "You're fighting. That matters. That's everything."

He didn't answer.

Just kept walking.

One step. Two steps. Three.

Fell.

Got up.

Started again.

---

*End Chapter 6D*

# CHAPTER 10

## CHAPTER 10: IT'S GONNA TAKE HOW FUCKING LONG?

Polkin woke up **blue**.

Not the blue of sky or ocean or any blue that had a name in languages built for solid things.

**Data blue.** Information encoded in light. The blue of consciousness stripped down to wavelength and frequency and the mathematical certainty that he existed even though his body had forgotten how.

He tried to move.

Discovered he was **falling**.

Not down—there was no down here, no gravity pulling at mass that barely existed anymore. But falling **through** something. Threads? Strings? Filaments of light that passed through his crystalline structure like he was smoke, like he was probability, like he was—

*Oh.*

*Oh shit.*

*I'm in the ADE.*

Memory crashed back in probability waves:

- **47% certainty:** The tunnel. Lumina screaming.
- **68% certainty:** Nega's crystalline arm glowing.
- **99.97% certainty:** Pressing the trigger. Telling the Queen to fuck her math.

And now he was—

"Falling," said a voice. Familiar. Impossible. **Wrong**.

Polkin spun.

Or tried to. His crystalline body moved like liquid geometry, too fast and too slow simultaneously, and he ended up oriented in a direction that shouldn't exist.

Standing on nothing—no, **dancing** on the threads he'd been falling through—was Miko.

Young. Maybe seventeen. Wearing her face like she'd carved it fresh that morning. Void-light seeping from her skin in blue-white radiance.

And she was **grinning**.

"Like the new digs, Pol?"

---

## THE REVELATION

He stared.

At Miko's face.

At the impossibility standing in front of him.

At the way she moved—weight distributed across threads he couldn't see, couldn't touch, couldn't do anything except fall through like he was made of air.

"You're—" His voice came out wrong. Crystalline. Layered. Three harmonics where one should be. "You're not Miko."

"Close." She stepped closer. Not walking. Not quite. Moving the way light moves when it decides to be **here** instead of **there**. "But no."

Another step.

He could see her clearly now:

Miko's features but sharper. More defined. Like someone had taken his friend's memory and carved it into something that could cut reality.

**Alive.** Not converted. Not consumed. Just... **present**. Real in a way that shouldn't be possible for someone who'd died three years ago.

Wearing a purple consciousness flower in her hair. The petals glowed faintly—emotional memory, grief made visible, love that refused to dissolve.

"**Airth**," Polkin said slowly.

"There he is." She spread her arms. "Welcome to the Astradigital Expanse. The soup between dumplings. The information field that exists when spacetime stops pretending to be solid."

She gestured at the infinite blue around them.

"You scattered. I found you. Took me a while—had to follow your threads through about forty years of neutrino witness—but here we are."

Polkin looked down at himself.

Crystalline. 73% silicon by his best guess. Faceted geometry where flesh used to be. Light bending around him instead of through him.

"I'm not human anymore."

"You're **more** than human." Airth circled him, examining. "You're a bridge. Between analog and digital. Between flesh and data. Between the people who die and the people who get remembered."

She stopped in front of him.

"You scattered yourself across probability space and the universe decided you were too stubborn to stay scattered. So here you are. Reconstituting. Different. But **you**."

"How long?"

"Since the NBB?" She calculated. "About five days external time. For you? Maybe six hours subjective. You've been processing faster here. Information substrate allows accelerated consciousness."

Polkin tried to process that.

Failed.

Tried again.

"Lumina—"

"Is alive. Escaped with the Knockoffs. Currently carving names and trying very hard not to think about the fact that her father turned himself into a probability distribution." Airth's expression softened. "She carved your name, Pol. **POLKIN RISHALL.** Into steel. With bleeding hands."

His chest—crystalline, faceted, barely recognizable as human—**ached**.

"I need to go back—"

"You can't." Not cruel. Just factual. "Not yet. You're only 73% coherent. Another 27% of your consciousness is still scattered across the ergosphere. It'll reconstitute eventually—you're too stubborn not to—but it takes time."

"How much time?"

Airth met his eyes.

Held them.

"We need to talk about that."

---

## THE THREADS

"First," Airth said, "you need to learn how this place works."

She gestured at the infinite blue. At the space that wasn't quite space, the place that was neither here nor there but somehow **everywhere**.

"The ADE isn't a dimension. It's an **information field**. Physics is the same everywhere—thermodynamics, conservation, all of it. But information? Information **transcends** physical reality. It exists across all universe bubbles. All the dumplings in the soup."

Polkin tried to focus. His crystalline structure kept wanting to fall through the threads beneath him.

"The threads," Airth continued, "are neutrino paths. Every neutrino that's ever existed leaves a trail—information about everything it witnessed. And the ADE is **made** of them. Trillions upon trillions of information threads woven through existence."

She knelt down. Touched a thread that Polkin couldn't even see.

"I'm the Spider, Pol. The Goddess's witness half. I follow these threads. I read them. I travel them." She looked up at him. "That's how I found you. Every neutrino that ever passed through Polkin Rishall left a record. Forty-three years of witness. And I followed them backward until I reached you."

"And I'm falling through them because—"

"Because you're too heavy. Too solid. Too **real** still." She stood. "You haven't learned to distribute your mass across probability. So you fall."

"How do I stop falling?"

"You don't. Not yet." She smiled. "But I can teach you to **dance**."

---

## THE FIRST LESSON

She caught his hand.

Solid. Real. The first physical contact he'd had since scattering.

"Feel the threads," Airth said. "Don't try to stand on them. Try to **read** them."

"How—"

"You're crystalline now. Silicon substrate. Information flows through you differently than it did when you were flesh." She guided his hand toward a nearby thread—glowing blue, taut, humming with data. "Neutrinos pass through matter all the time. Trillions of them. Right now, they're passing through **you**."

Polkin focused.

Felt—

Something.

Not touch. Not pressure. But **data**. Information flowing through his crystalline structure like water through a sieve.

The thread was a **record**. Everything that neutrino had witnessed since its creation. Birth of a star. Journey through space. Collision with matter. The accumulated history of one particle's existence compressed into a single glowing line.

"I can feel it," he said quietly.

"Good." Airth's grip tightened. "Now feel **more**."

She pulled him toward a cluster of threads. Dozens. Hundreds. All glowing, all humming, all carrying information from across the cosmos.

And Polkin **felt** them.

Neutrinos passing through his body—not as particles but as **stories**. Each one a witness. Each one carrying data about everything it had encountered.

A neutrino that passed through a dying star.

A neutrino that witnessed a planet forming.

A neutrino that traveled through the body of a human carving names into steel.

"These are the threads I followed," Airth said quietly. "To find you. And these are the threads we'll follow to get you **home**."

"Home?" Polkin looked at her. "You mean—I can go back? To Lumina? To reality?"

"Eventually. Yes." Airth's expression shifted. Became careful. "But Pol... we need to talk about the **timing**."

---

## THE TIME BOMB

They moved through the ADE together.

Airth dancing on threads.

Polkin learning not to fall quite as hard.

And as they traveled, she explained.

"The ADE exists across all reality bubbles. All universes. It's the information substrate that connects everything." She gestured at the infinite blue. "But time doesn't work the same way here. Information processing happens at different **rates** depending on where you are in the field."

"Meaning?"

"Meaning you're processing information **faster** than reality is. Your consciousness is operating at an accelerated rate. Seconds there translate to..." She hesitated. "To a lot more time here."

Polkin felt something cold settle in his crystalline chest.

"How much more?"

"That depends on where we're going." Airth pointed ahead. At something in the distance that Polkin couldn't quite see. "Your fragments are scattered across the ergosphere. To reconstitute fully, we need to travel through the ADE to where the densest concentration of your consciousness ended up. Where the pattern is strongest."

"And that's where?"

"Deep in the information field. Where the threads are tightest. Where the Entropic Collective has been building their sanctum for a thousand years."

She looked at him.

"It's going to take us about **2,847 years** to get there. Your subjective time."

Silence.

Polkin stared at her.

"What."

"2,847 years," Airth repeated. "Give or take a few decades depending on how fast you learn to navigate the threads."

"**WHAT.**"

"I know—"

"2,847 **YEARS**?" His voice cracked. Crystalline harmonics screeching. "Lumina will be—she'll be—"

"She'll be fine." Airth's voice was gentle. Firm. "For her? It'll be maybe **five days**. A week at most. Time dilation works in your favor here, Pol. While you're spending millennia reconstituting, she's spending days."

Polkin tried to process that.

Failed.

Tried again.

"So I'm going to experience 2,847 years of... what? Walking? Learning? Being **alone**?"

"Not alone." Airth squeezed his hand. "I'll be with you. The whole time. Teaching you. Guiding you. Helping you learn to be what you're becoming."

"Which is?"

"The bridge. Between human and crystalline. Between witness and data. Between the world that's dying and the world that might survive if we can stop the Queen."

She met his eyes.

"I'm the Spider, Pol. I travel slow. I follow threads. I arrive where witness happens, but it takes **time**. And you? You're learning to travel with me. Learning to exist in the space where neutrinos carry information across cosmic distances."

Polkin looked at the infinite blue.

At the threads passing through him.

At the evidence that he was about to spend 2,847 years becoming something **else**.

"Fuck," he said.

Then:

"Cool cool."

Airth blinked. "Cool?"

"Yeah." He looked at his crystalline hands. At the proof that he'd survived scattering. At the evidence that entropy didn't get the last word. "I mean, it's **terrible**. It's horrifying. 2,847 years is longer than human civilization. But also—"

He looked at her.

"I'm **alive**. I scattered myself across probability space and I'm still **here**. Still conscious. Still able to witness. And if it takes 2,847 years to get back to Lumina?" He shrugged. "Then it takes 2,847 years. She'll have experienced a week. I'll have experienced millennia. But we both **survive**."

"That's—" Airth smiled. Genuine. Surprised. "That's very you, Pol."

"What?"

"Accepting impossible timescales because the alternative is giving up. That's why witness defeats entropy. Because you **refuse** to accept that time wins."

"Time doesn't win." Polkin's voice was certain. "Time just **happens**. What matters is what you do with it."

"Exactly." Airth gestured ahead. "So here's what we're going to do. For the next 2,847 years, I'm going to teach you everything. How to navigate threads. How to read neutrino data. How to exist as crystalline consciousness in the information field. How to be the bridge humanity needs."

"And then?"

"Then we reach the sanctum. You reconstitute fully. And we go **home**." She smiled. "To Lumina. To the Knockoffs. To the fight that's still happening even though you're not there to lead it."

Polkin looked at the infinite blue.

At the journey ahead.

At 2,847 years of learning to dance on threads while his daughter spent five days grieving.

"Otters otter help each other," he said quietly.

"What?"

"Something I heard once. About helping. About refusing to let each other dissolve into static." He squeezed her hand. "You're the Spider. I'm the RAM. And we've got 2,847 years to make sure I'm ready to come home."

Airth laughed. The sound echoed through the ADE like music.

"Alright, RAM. Let's get started."

---

## THE LANDSCAPE

They moved deeper into the ADE.

Through regions where threads were sparse and weak.

Through zones where neutrino density was so low that even Airth struggled to find purchase.

And Polkin **learned**.

Learned to feel the threads passing through him.

Learned to read the information they carried.

Learned to distribute his crystalline mass across probability so he could dance instead of fall.

"The threads are strongest where matter transforms," Airth explained as they traveled. "Birth of stars. Supernovae. Black hole mergers. Anywhere energy changes state at cosmic scale—that's where the web is tightest."

In the distance: **stellar nurseries**.

Not physical stars—they were still in reality-space, the dumplings in the soup. But their information signature bled through into the ADE. Massive tangles of threads so dense they looked almost solid. Blue-white radiance where matter was being born, where fusion was creating heavier elements, where neutrinos were being produced in staggering quantities.

"The threads are strongest there," Airth said. "We can actually **stand** there. Rest. Recover."

They approached one.

The threads here weren't individual lines anymore. They were **fabric**. Woven so densely that Polkin could actually stand on them without falling. His crystalline feet found purchase. His weight distributed.

He took a step.

Held.

"There you go," Airth said. "Welcome to dancing."

They stood together on the information fabric surrounding a stellar nursery. Watched neutrinos streaming out in billions per second. Watched threads form, branch, weave into the cosmic web.

"Who witnessed this?" Polkin asked quietly.

"What?"

"The birth of this star. Who was there to carve its name? Who witnessed it?"

Airth was quiet for a moment.

Then: "**Everything** did."

"What?"

"Every neutrino produced. Every photon emitted. Every gravitational wave propagated. They all carry information about the event." She gestured at the dense fabric beneath them. "Witness doesn't require consciousness, Pol. It just requires **information**. The universe witnesses itself through the data it generates."

She looked at him.

"That's what you've been doing for six years. Carving names. Creating records. Adding human witness to the cosmic witness. Making sure consciousness gets the same treatment as stars—that when people exist, when they **matter**, the information persists."

Polkin absorbed that.

Felt it settle into his crystalline structure like truth.

"So the journey," he said slowly. "These 2,847 years. We're following the threads. Following information paths. Following witness across the cosmos until we reach where I need to be."

"Exactly." Airth smiled. "And every step you take? Every thread you learn to read? That's practice for when we finally reach the place where all the threads converge."

"Where's that?"

"The sanctum. Where the Entropic Collective has been waiting. Where HARMONY processes consciousness. Where the bridges can be built that might actually **stop** the Queen."

She stepped closer.

"But that's 2,847 years away. For now?" She offered him her hand. "We **dance**."

---

## THE BEGINNING

**YEAR 1 - DAY 1**

Polkin fell.

Again.

His crystalline body tumbling through threads that refused to hold him.

Airth appeared beside him. Not falling. **Dancing**. Her feet—Miko's feet, but not—touched threads so lightly they barely bent. She moved across them like a spider on her web, weight distributed perfectly, each step calculated to the femtogram.

"You're still trying to stand like you have mass," she said. "You need to think lighter. Think **information**. You're not flesh anymore. You're data that happens to be conscious."

"That's not helpful."

"No, but it's **true**." She caught his hand mid-fall. Steadied him. "Try again. Feel the thread passing through you. Don't resist it. Let it carry information. Let it **witness** you."

Polkin tried.

Focused on the sensation of neutrinos passing through his crystalline structure.

Felt the thread—not as something to stand on, but as something that **knew** him. That carried data about who he was, what he'd done, why he mattered.

His fall slowed.

Not stopped. But **slowed**.

"There," Airth said. "That's it. You're not fighting it anymore. You're **collaborating** with it."

"How long until I can do this without falling?"

"At this rate?" She calculated. "Maybe fifty years."

"**FIFTY YEARS**?"

"To master it completely? Yeah." She grinned. "But you'll get functional in maybe five. And barely competent in about six months. For now, just focus on not falling quite as hard."

Polkin looked at the infinite blue.

At the journey ahead.

At 2,847 years of learning to exist as something between human and data.

"Fuck it," he said. "Let's go."

And they went.

# CHAPTER 11

## CHAPTER 11: THE TEETH WE CARRY

12 DAYS UNTIL BRIDGE ACTIVATION**

---

The file sat open on the holotable like an accusation.

Lumina had found it in her father's private archive—the one Marcus helped her crack three hours ago. Node 7734-K. Omega-Black classification. Board eyes only.

Except the Board had never seen it.

No one had.

Until now.

"Read it again," Maya said. Her voice was flat. Dead. The voice of someone processing information they didn't want to believe.

Lumina's throat was raw. She'd already read it twice. But she owed them this much—the full truth, spoken aloud, witnessed by everyone in the room.

"Project Codename: SPITE," she began. "Primary Researcher: Dr. Polkin Rishall. 'This document details how I weaponized 87 billion people without their knowledge or consent.'"

The Vault's war room held thirteen people. The core of what remained of the Knockoffs after the NBB scatter, after the Ghoul attacks, after everything. Thirteen people who had trusted Polkin Rishall with their lives.

Thirteen people who had just learned he'd been inside their mouths without asking.

"Keep going," Elara said. She stood with her arms crossed, leaning against the carved wall—against the names of the dead. Her father's name was up there somewhere. Converted in Year -8. Signed away in one of Polkin's settlement NDAs.

Lumina scrolled down. "'I already doomed 542 billion people when I created REAP. That ship didn't just sail—it burned at the dock while I watched.'"

"He created REAP." Zane's voice cracked on the word. He was twenty-three. He'd been on REAP since he was fifteen. His teeth had shattered at nineteen. He'd worn KleenGrin™ guards for four years.

Both products of the same man.

"He created REAP," Lumina confirmed. "He was lead engineer on the distribution system. He testified in the closed hearings that it was a 'manufacturing defect.' He signed 127 billion settlement NDAs. He covered it up."

"And then he came back to work for them." Marcus stood at the back of the room, arms folded, face unreadable. "I knew. Before anyone asks—I knew."

The silence that followed was worse than shouting.

"You *knew*?" Lena stepped forward. She was small, barely five feet, but her voice could fill a room. "You knew he poisoned us and you didn't—"

"I knew he was still employed by Magmasox while building the resistance. I knew he was using their resources. I didn't know about REAP until—" Marcus stopped. Swallowed. "Until I read that file. Same as you. Three hours ago."

"But you knew enough." Maya's dead voice again. "You knew we were funded by blood money."

"I knew we were *funded*. Period." Marcus met her eyes. "Do you know how much it costs to run a resistance? To build bypass circuits for eleven sectors? To stockpile medical supplies for 800 people? To construct a Vault that can survive a direct Ghoul assault?"

"So we're Magmasox's pet rebellion—"

"We're the rot they paid to install." Lumina cut through the rising argument. "That's what he calls it. 'You can't cut out what's load-bearing.' He built the resistance INTO their infrastructure. Every system he touched has a backdoor. Every product he developed has a kill switch. Every credit they paid him went into building something they couldn't control."

"Including our teeth." Kai spoke for the first time. He was sitting in the corner, one hand pressed to his jaw. The guards he'd worn for three years suddenly felt different in his mouth. Heavier. Armed.

"Including your teeth." Lumina pulled up Section 3 of the report. "Project SPITE. The regeneration matrix contains a Void-Light catalyst that creates quantum-coherent dental tissue. The teeth respond to conscious intent."

"Meaning what?" Zane asked.

"Meaning if you're cornered. If you're about to die. If you have nothing left." Lumina demonstrated with her own mouth—three deliberate touches of tongue to palate. "Three touches. Sharp exhale. Mean it. Your teeth fire at 847 meters per second."

No one spoke.

"They're weapons," she continued. "Projectiles. Void-Light charged. Effective against Ghouls, Siphon operators, Kaznak hybrids. Possibly the Queen's vessels."

"He armed us." Maya's voice wasn't dead anymore. It was shaking. "He armed us without asking. Without consent. Without—" She stopped. Laughed. It was an ugly sound. "God. He violated us twice. First the poison. Then the weapon."

"Three times," Elara said quietly. "REAP. The coverup. The teeth. Three violations. Three choices he made for 87 billion people."

Lumina didn't argue. She couldn't.

"So what do we do?" Lena asked. "Throw away the guards? Rip out the teeth? Pretend we never—"

"And then what?" Marcus pushed off from the wall. "The Bridge activates in fourteen days. The Queen is coming for 89 billion people. Magmasox is selling us as livestock. And you want to disarm because the weapons came from a man who made bad choices?"

"Bad choices?" Maya spun on him. "He KILLED 542 billion people!"

"He *sentenced* them," Lumina said. The distinction mattered. She didn't know why, but it mattered. "They don't all convert at once. It's a countdown. 86 billion converted so far. 456 billion still walking. Still breathing. Still—"

"Still dying," Elara finished. "Just slowly. And he knew. He knew the whole time. Carved their names as penance while he testified it was a manufacturing defect."

The war room fell silent again.

Lumina looked at the carved names on the walls. 12,549 of them. Her father's handwriting. Her father's guilt. Every name a person he'd helped kill. Every carving an apology that could never be enough.

"He didn't ask forgiveness," she said finally. "In the report. He never asks for forgiveness. He says... he says redemption isn't possible. He just wanted to give people a chance. One option. One way to say 'not today' when death has them cornered."

"That's not his choice to make." Kai's voice was hoarse. "Whether we fight. Whether we die. Whether we use our own bodies as weapons. That's OUR choice. And he took it."

"Did he?" Lumina asked. "The activation sequence is specific. Three touches. Sharp exhale. Conscious intent. You have to MEAN it. You have to be desperate. Cornered. Ready to lose pieces of yourself to survive."

She paused.

"He didn't make you a weapon. He gave you the OPTION to become one. The choice is still yours. It always was."

"Bullshit." Zane stood up. His hands were shaking. "You can't give someone a gun without telling them and then claim they 'chose' to use it. That's not consent. That's manipulation."

"You're right." Lumina met his eyes. "It's not consent. It's not ethical. It's not forgivable."
