"Then why are you defending him?"

"I'm not."

The words came out harder than she intended. She took a breath.

"I'm not defending him. I'm telling you what he did. What he was. A war criminal who tried to die a hero. A poisoner who became a carver. A father who sold his soul to Magmasox twice—once to poison you, once to try to save you."

She looked around the room. At the faces of people who had trusted her father. Who had followed him into impossible odds. Who had worn his guards and slept with his weapons in their mouths for years.

"He was all of it. The monster and the savior. The problem and the solution. And I can't make that okay. I can't carve redemption into his name because he doesn't deserve it."

"Then what DO we do?" Lena asked again.

Lumina pulled up the distribution numbers. 86.8 billion guards distributed. 82.7% coverage of REAP users. An army that didn't know it was an army.

"The Bridge activates in fourteen days," she said. "When it does, there's an automated broadcast. Dad encoded it into the guard's neural interface. It'll tell everyone how to activate. How to fight back. How to—"

"Rinse and spit," Marcus finished. He'd read the file too. "That's his message. Rinse and spit."

"It's fucking insane," Zane said. "You're talking about 87 billion people firing their teeth at Magmasox enforcers. At Ghouls. At whatever the Queen sends through that Bridge. It'll be chaos. Slaughter."

"It'll be SOMETHING." Maya's voice had changed again. Not dead. Not shaking. Something harder. "It'll be spite. 87 billion people saying 'not today' instead of dying quietly."

"You're okay with this?" Elara stared at her. "You were just screaming about consent—"

"I'm not okay with ANY of this!" Maya shouted. "I'm not okay with REAP! I'm not okay with the coverup! I'm not okay with being armed without asking! But I'm ALSO not okay with being LIVESTOCK!"

She grabbed the holotable, knuckles white.

"We're going to die, Elara. All of us. The countdown is running. The Bridge is building. The Queen is hungry. And the ONLY thing standing between 89 billion people and total erasure is a dead man's spite."

"A scattered man," Lumina corrected quietly. "He's not dead. He's scattered. And he's still out there. Searching for me."

"Then he can live with what he did." Maya met her eyes. "He can live with the 542 billion. With the coverup. With the teeth. He can carry that weight while we carry his weapons."

"And if we use them?" Kai asked. "If we rinse and spit? We become part of it. Part of HIS choice. His violation."

"We become survivors." Marcus moved to the holotable. Pulled up the tactical display—the Bridge construction site, the Synchronization Array, the fourteen days of impossible odds. "Survivors who didn't go quietly. Survivors who bit back."

Lena laughed. It wasn't an ugly sound this time. Just tired. "Bit back. Jesus. That's what we're calling it? We're going to shoot our teeth at the Queen and call it 'biting back'?"

"Would you prefer 'spitting defiance'?" Zane asked. A ghost of a smile crossed his face. "Rinse and resist?"

"I prefer not dying," Lena said. "I prefer the Queen choking on us instead of swallowing us whole."

Lumina watched them. Watched the horror shift into dark humor. Watched the betrayal curdle into determination. It wasn't acceptance—not yet, maybe not ever. But it was something.

It was enough.

"We have fourteen days," she said. "Dad left us more than teeth. He left backdoors. Kill switches. Everything in his archive, everything he built at Magmasox—it's all weapons. All sabotage. All aimed at the people who thought they owned him."

"And we're going to use it." Marcus nodded. "All of it."

"Even knowing where it came from?" Elara asked. Her voice had lost its edge. She sounded exhausted. "Even knowing what he did?"

Lumina looked at the carved names on the wall. Found the one she'd carved herself, three days ago, with Kai's help.

POLKIN RISHALL.

Not the full epitaph—not yet. She'd finish it when this was over. When she knew whether spite was enough.

"Even knowing," she said. "Because spite is all we have left."

Maya touched her jaw. Ran her tongue along her teeth—the weapons she'd carried for years without knowing.

"Three touches," she said quietly. "Sharp exhale. Mean it."

"Mean it," Lumina confirmed.

"And if we survive?"

Lumina thought about the 456 billion people still walking. Still breathing. Still counting down to conversion. About the 87 billion armed mouths and the 12,549 preserved souls. About a father who poisoned the world and then tried to teach it to bite.

"If we survive," she said, "we carve his name true. All of it. The good and the bad. The poison and the cure. We don't forget what he did. We don't forgive it."

"But we use it," Marcus finished.

"We use it."

Zane stood. Touched his jaw. The guards in his mouth—the weapons his poisoner had given him—felt different now. Heavier. More real.

"Rinse and spit," he said.

One by one, the others echoed it. A dark mantra. A promise of spite.

"Rinse and spit."

"Rinse and spit."

"Rinse and spit."

Lumina listened to them claim her father's legacy. The poison and the weapon. The violation and the choice.

"Wait." Lena raised her hand. The room had been chanting; now it was all ears.

"What happens when we fire? What does it feel like?" she asked.

Lumina scrolled through the file and found the section marked **"Regrowth Cycle - The Cost."**

"You lose the teeth," she read aloud. "Bleeding sockets for days. Six to eight weeks of liquid diet. You can't eat solids for a month."

She looked up at their faces. "And everyone will know what you did. A gap-toothed fighter. The mark of someone who chose to bite back."

"The teeth regrow if you keep wearing the guards," she continued. "But they're different each time—different color, different sensitivity, slightly crooked. You're physically marked by the choice."

Silence settled like dust.

Zane let out a humorless laugh. "So we lose pieces of ourselves to survive."

"Yeah," Lumina said. "Just like he did."

Maya touched her jaw, tongue brushing the ridge where a fired tooth would have been. "Then we lose them *loud*," she said.

Another silence. This one understanding.

Somewhere out there, scattered across probability space, Polkin Rishall was still searching for her.

She hoped he could hear them.

She hoped it hurt.

---

*Later.*

The war room emptied slowly. People needed time alone. Time to process. Time to touch their teeth and wonder what they'd become.

Marcus stayed behind. Waited until it was just him and Lumina.

"You okay?"

Lumina laughed. It sounded like her mother's laugh—the bitter one, the one that meant nothing was okay and might never be again.

"My father poisoned half a trillion people. Covered it up. Weaponized the cure. Funded the resistance with blood money. And scattered himself across probability space looking for me."

She met Marcus's eyes.

"I'm supposed to carve his name when this is over. But I don't know what to carve. Hero? Villain? Monster? Savior?"

Marcus was quiet for a long moment.

"Carve the truth," he said finally. "All of it. Let the people who survive decide what it means."

"And if no one survives?"

"Then the names on these walls are the only record that we existed. That we fought. That we spat in death's face and refused to go quietly."

Lumina looked at the walls. 12,549 names. Her father's handwriting. Her father's guilt.

Her inheritance.

"He used to tell me that carving names was about witnessing," she said. "Preserving someone's existence against entropy. Making sure they weren't forgotten."

"And now?"

"Now I think it was penance. Every name—every person he carved—was someone he helped kill. Someone whose death he testified was a 'manufacturing defect.' Someone whose family he paid off with blood money."

She touched the wall. Felt the grooves beneath her fingers.

"He wasn't witnessing them. He was apologizing to them. And he knew it would never be enough."

Marcus had no answer for that.

Neither did she.

---

*The holotable stayed active all night.*

Lumina read her father's files until her eyes burned. The technical specifications. The distribution numbers. The Kaznak science that made quantum-coherent teeth possible.

And the messages he'd left at the end.

*To Magmasox: You paid me to arm your victims.*

*To the Queen: Welcome to the resistance. It tastes like spite.*

*To Lumina: Carve my name true.*

She would.

When this was over—when the Bridge fell or humanity did—she would carve every word. Scientist. Poisoner. Carver. War Criminal. Resistance Leader. Father. Weapon.

All of it.

The good and the bad and the unforgivable.

Because that's what witnessing meant.

Not forgetting.

Not forgiving.

Just... remembering.

The whole truth.

Even when it hurt.

---

**END CHAPTER 11**

---

*Author's Note: The teeth we carry are not always the teeth we chose. Sometimes they're weapons. Sometimes they're poison. Sometimes they're both. The question isn't whether we deserved them—it's whether we'll use them when death comes knocking.*

*Rinse and spit.*

*Not today, motherfucker.*

# CHAPTER 12

## CHAPTER 12: MOUTHS FULL OF MURDER

10 DAYS UNTIL BRIDGE ACTIVATION**

---

Nobody slept.

The file had been closed for six hours. The holotable was dark. The war room was empty except for the ghosts of what they'd learned.

But nobody slept.

Lumina found Maya in the lower storage level at 03:00, sitting with her back against a supply crate, staring at her hands.

"Hey."

Maya didn't look up. "Hey."

Lumina sat beside her. Close enough for presence, far enough for space. The way her father had taught her.

Silence stretched between them like taffy.

"I keep touching my teeth," Maya finally said. Her voice was hollow. Wrong. "I keep... running my tongue along them. Feeling the edges. Wondering which ones are—" She broke off. Swallowed hard. "Which ones are the bullets."

Lumina didn't have an answer. There wasn't one.

"The file said all of them," Maya continued. "Every single tooth. 847 meters per second. That's faster than most projectile weapons. That's faster than—" Her voice cracked. "I'm a gun. We're all guns. And nobody asked us."

"I know."

"Do you?" Maya finally looked at her. Eyes red. Face wet. "Because you seem really fucking calm about having murder in your mouth."

Lumina considered lying.

Decided not to.

"I threw up," she said quietly. "After everyone left the war room. In the bathroom behind the armory. Twice."

Maya blinked.

"Then I washed my face, looked in the mirror, and smiled. Just to see what it looked like now. What *I* looked like." Lumina's jaw tightened. "I couldn't stop smiling. For about ten minutes. Just... staring at my teeth and smiling and thinking about what they could do."

"That's fucked up."

"Yeah."

"Really fucked up."

"I know."

Silence again. But different this time. Shared.

"Does it help?" Maya asked. "Knowing we can fight back?"

Lumina thought about it. Really thought.

"No," she admitted. "It just adds a new kind of horror to the pile."

---

## THE WEIGHT OF ENAMEL

Marcus found Zane in the Vault proper at 04:30.

He was standing in front of the wall of names, fingers hovering over the carved letters, not quite touching. His lips were moving. Counting.

"How many?" Marcus asked.

"Twelve thousand, five hundred and forty-nine." Zane's hand dropped. "Give or take. Some of the older carvings are hard to read."

"That's not what you were counting."

Zane was quiet for a long moment.

"I was counting how many times I've smiled at someone," he finally said. "Since the REAP exposure. Since the KleenGrin. Every time I opened my mouth and showed my teeth and didn't know I was showing them a weapon."

"That's a lot of smiles."

"Thousands." Zane turned. His face was pale in the void-light. Wrong. "I smiled at my mother. At her funeral. Trying to be strong for my little sister. And I had—" He gestured at his mouth. "I had *these* the whole time. Pointed at everyone I loved."

Marcus didn't have comfort to offer. Comfort felt like a lie right now.

"Did you know?" Zane asked. "Before the file. Did you have any idea?"

"No."

"Not even a suspicion?"

"I knew Polkin was hiding something." Marcus leaned against the wall, careful not to touch the names. "I always knew. The way he looked at us sometimes—like we were something fragile and dangerous at the same time. I thought it was guilt about REAP. About what Magmasox did to everyone."

"But it was this."

"It was this."

Zane's laugh was bitter. Broken. "He armed us without asking. Turned us into weapons without consent. And we're supposed to be grateful because it might save our lives?"

"I don't think he expected gratitude."

"What did he expect?"

Marcus thought about Polkin. About the man who'd carved names for six years. Who'd built the Vault. Who'd scattered himself across probability to save them.

Who'd put guns in their mouths and never said a word.

"I think he expected us to hate him," Marcus said quietly. "I think he was counting on it."

---

## THE CONVERSATION THEY WEREN'T HAVING

Elara's workshop hummed with activity at 05:15.

Not productive activity. The kind of activity that happens when someone can't stop moving because stopping means thinking.

"You should sleep," Kai said from the doorway.

Elara didn't look up from her diagnostic scanner. "You should mind your own business."

"Fair." He walked in anyway, pulling up a stool. "What are you working on?"

"Trying to figure out the activation mechanism." She gestured at a holographic display showing a tooth cross-section. Layers of enamel. Crystalline matrices. Quantum-coherent structures that shouldn't be possible in organic tissue. "Touch. Touch. Touch. Exhale. Intent. That's what the file said."

"Sounds complicated."

"It's not." Elara's voice was flat. "That's the problem. It's terrifyingly simple. Three deliberate touches to the same tooth. A sharp exhale. And *meaning it*."

"Meaning what?"

"*Meaning it*." She finally looked at him. Her eyes were red-rimmed. Exhausted. Haunted. "Wanting to hurt something. Wanting to *end* something. The quantum states only collapse into projectile form if there's genuine lethal intent behind them."

Kai processed that.

"So you can't fire by accident."

"No. You can only fire on purpose." Elara laughed—hollow, wrong. "It's almost elegant. A safety mechanism built into the murder. You have to choose to kill. Every single time."

"That's... something, I guess."

"Is it?" She stood abruptly, pacing. "Because I keep thinking about every time I've been angry. Every time I've wanted to hurt someone, even for a second. What if my finger had slipped? What if I'd touched my tooth wrong while thinking about someone I hated?"

"The file said it requires intent."

"The file said it requires *lethal* intent. How do you measure that? How do you know when you've crossed the line from 'I want to punch you' to 'I want you dead'?" She stopped. Stared at the hologram. "What if it's closer than we think?"

Kai didn't have an answer.

Neither did she.

---

## THE ONES WHO COULDN'T STOP CRYING

Lena found three of the newer refugees in the medical bay at 06:00.

They were huddled in the corner, arms wrapped around each other, sobbing. Not the quiet crying of grief—the loud, ugly, uncontrollable crying of people who had just discovered something unbearable.

She knew exactly what they'd discovered.

"Hey," she said softly, approaching slowly. "Hey. It's okay. You're safe."

One of them looked up—a woman about Lena's age, blonde hair plastered to tear-streaked cheeks. "How can you say that? How can you say we're *safe* when we're—when we have—"

"I know."

"You don't know. You can't—"

"I have them too." Lena crouched down, putting herself at their level. "The teeth. The weapon. The whole thing. I found out last night with everyone else."

The woman's crying stuttered. "You... you're one of the Knockoffs. You're supposed to protect us."

"I'm trying."

"But you're a weapon. How can a weapon protect anyone?"

Lena thought about her own reaction. The long hours staring at the ceiling. The urge to rip her own teeth out, one by one, just to be clean again.

The slow, awful realization that it wouldn't help.

"I don't know," she admitted. "I really don't. But I know that the weapon isn't who we are. It's just... something we have. Like a knife in a drawer. It's there, but we choose what to do with it."

"I didn't choose this."

"Neither did I." Lena reached out, touched the woman's arm. Gentle. Careful. "None of us did. But we're still here. We're still *us*. And maybe that's what matters."

The woman's crying didn't stop.

But it quieted.

---

## THE ONE WHO COULDN'T STOP TOUCHING

Lumina found herself in front of a mirror at 07:00.

The bathroom in the lower levels was empty this early. Gray light filtered through the ventilation grates. The recycled air tasted like metal and regret.

She opened her mouth.

Looked at her teeth.

White. Straight. Normal-looking. The result of two years of dental work when she was twelve—before the Queen, before the garden, before everything went wrong.

KleenGrin™ had fixed them. The report had said so. Accelerated regeneration. Perfect enamel. Cosmetically flawless.

And underneath: quantum-coherent crystalline matrices. Void-light channels. Enough kinetic potential to punch through a Ghoul's skull at close range.

She touched her front tooth.

Once.

Her finger trembled.

*That's one,* she thought. *Two more and an exhale and wanting someone dead and I become a killer in a way I can never take back.*

She dropped her hand.

Closed her mouth.

Stared at her reflection.

"My father did this," she said to the mirror. "He put this in me. In all of us. Eighty-seven billion people. And he never said anything."

The mirror didn't answer.

"He saved us. At the NBB. Scattered himself to buy us time." Her voice cracked. "And he was also the man who turned us into weapons without asking. How am I supposed to—"

She broke off.

Touched her tooth again.

Twice now.

One more touch. One exhale. One moment of genuine lethal intent.

And she'd know.

She'd *know* what it felt like. What it could do. Whether the horror was worth it.

Her hand hovered over her mouth.

Her chest tightened.

And she stepped back.

Not today.

Not yet.

Maybe not ever.

Some knowledge was better left theoretical.

---

## THE MORNING AFTER

The war room filled slowly at 08:00.

Nobody looked rested. Everyone looked older than they had yesterday.

Marcus stood at the head of the table, waiting until all twelve Knockoffs were present. Lumina sat in her usual spot, Kai beside her—close, but not touching. Maya across the table, Zane by the door. Elara at her terminal. The others scattered around the room like survivors of a shipwreck.

"We need to talk about it," Marcus said.

Nobody spoke.

"I know you're processing. I know this is—" He stopped. Started again. "There's no good way to say this. We have weapons in our mouths. All of us. Eighty-seven billion people across ten planets. Polkin did this, and he didn't tell us, and now he's scattered across the ADE and we can't even ask him why."

"I know why." Lumina's voice was quiet. Steady. "The file said why. He called it 'spite.' He knew we were going to die—the harvest, the Queen, the whole thing. And he wanted us to be able to fight back. Even if it was just one shot. Even if it killed us."

"That's not comfort," Maya said.

"It's not supposed to be." Lumina looked around the table. "Nothing about this is comforting. We're weapons. We didn't consent. And now we have to decide what to do with that."

"What *can* we do?" Zane asked. "We can't remove them. The file said extraction is fatal—the quantum matrices are integrated with our nervous systems."

"We don't remove them." Marcus straightened. "We accept them. Not happily. Not gratefully. But we accept that they're there, and we focus on what we *can* control."

"Which is?"

"Whether we use them." He looked at each of them in turn. "The activation requires intent. That means the choice is still ours. Always ours. Nobody fires unless they choose to."

"And if we choose to?" Elara asked. "What then?"

Silence.

"Then we lose them," Lumina said quietly. "The file was clear. One shot per tooth. And the regeneration doesn't restore the quantum matrices—just the enamel. Fire once, and you're one tooth closer to being normal."

"Normal." Maya laughed bitterly. "What even is that anymore?"

"I don't know." Lumina stood. "But I know we have a portal to steal, a Bridge to stop, and a Queen to fight. And right now, that's enough. The teeth can wait."

She walked toward the door.

Paused.

"For what it's worth," she said without turning around, "I'm glad we know. Even if it hurts. Even if it's horrible. The alternative is dying ignorant. And I'm done with secrets."

She left.

The room stayed quiet for a long time.

---

## THE RITUAL THEY DIDN'T PLAN

At 22:00, Lumina found them in the Vault proper.

All twelve Knockoffs. Standing in a loose circle around the wall of names. Silent. Waiting.

She didn't ask what they were doing. She knew.

"We should acknowledge it," Marcus said. His voice was rough. Tired. "Not celebrate. Not mourn. Just... acknowledge. What we are now. What we've been for years without knowing."

"A ritual," Elara said.

"A reckoning."

They stood in silence for a long moment.

Then Maya spoke:

"I have weapons in my mouth."

It wasn't a declaration. It wasn't a complaint. It was just... true.

"I have weapons in my mouth," Zane repeated.

"I have weapons in my mouth," Marcus said.

One by one, around the circle, they said it. The same words. The same truth. Until it reached Lumina.

She looked at the wall. At twelve thousand names carved into steel. At the legacy her father had left behind—preservation and destruction, witness and weapon, love and horror.

"I have weapons in my mouth," she said.

The words hung in the air.

Nobody moved.

"And I choose what I do with them," Lumina continued. "We all do. That's the only thing Polkin left us—the choice. Not whether we're weapons. But what we become."

Silence.

Then Maya, quietly: "Rinse."

Zane: "And spit."

The words spread around the circle. A mantra. A promise. A way of acknowledging the horror without drowning in it.

Rinse.

And spit.

Clean enough to look in the mirror.

Dirty enough to fight.

---

**STATUS BLOCK:**

```
VAULT POPULATION: 1,089 refugees
NAMES CARVED: 13,247 (and counting)
MORALE: Shattered but holding
TEETH: Untested (and staying that way)
NIGHTMARES: Universal
DAYS UNTIL BRIDGE ACTIVATION: 13
```

---

*Later that night, Lumina sat alone in her quarters, running her tongue along her teeth.*

*Counting them.*

*Thirty-two chances to kill.*

*Thirty-two choices she never asked for.*

*She touched her front tooth. Once. Deliberately.*

*Then she put her hand down.*

*Not today.*

*Probably not tomorrow.*

*But someday, maybe, if she had to, if there was no other choice—*

*She would know what her father had given her.*

*And she would make it count.*

---

**END CHAPTER 12**

# CHAPTER 13

## CHAPTER 13: THE LONGEST DAY

9 DAYS UNTIL BRIDGE ACTIVATION**

---

The war room had become a maze of holographic blueprints.

Sela's transmission had arrived at 06:00—encrypted, compressed, delivered through seventeen proxy relays that would take Magmasox weeks to trace. Inside: the complete architectural schematic of Facility 7, including the sublevel where Tral's "failed" portal prototype gathered dust.

"Four checkpoints," Marcus said, tracing the route with his finger. "Rotating patrols. Biometric locks. And—" He zoomed in. "Seventeen confirmed Ghouls in the lower levels."

"Confirmed?" Lumina asked.

"As of three days ago. Could be more by now."

"Could be less," Zane offered.

Everyone looked at him.

"Optimism," he said defensively. "It's a thing."

"Not for this team." Marcus turned back to the display. "We need a small footprint. Two people maximum. In, grab, out—no engagement if we can help it."

The room went quiet.

Lumina studied the blueprints. The ventilation system ran parallel to the secure corridors—old infrastructure, pre-Magmasox. The kind of thing that got overlooked because nobody thought to look.

"I'll go," she said.

Marcus didn't argue. They both knew she was the obvious choice. Six years of training. Six years of Vault operations. Six years of learning how to move through hostile territory without dying.

"You'll need backup," he said. "Someone who knows the inside of these facilities."

"I know the inside of—"

"Someone who knows them *without* trying to blow them up."

Lumina opened her mouth to argue.

"I'll go."

She turned.

Kai stood in the doorway, arms crossed, face unreadable. He'd been there for—how long? Long enough to hear the plan. Long enough to volunteer.

"You're not trained for infiltration," Zane said.

"I'm trained for not getting caught." Kai walked forward, studying the blueprints. "I used to run packages through Magmasox facilities. Before the Knockoffs. Before any of this. I know how they think. How they patrol. Where the blind spots are."

"Packages," Maya said flatly.

"Sensitive packages."

"Through ventilation shafts."

"Sometimes."

The room was quiet. Lumina could feel everyone's eyes on her. Waiting for her decision.

She should say no. She should insist on someone with combat experience. Someone she'd trained with, fought beside, trusted.

But there was something in Kai's expression. Something steady. Something that reminded her of—

*Don't finish that thought.*

"You've actually been inside a Magmasox facility?" she asked.

"Three times. Walked out every time."

"With the package?"

"With the package." He met her eyes. "I'm good at this. Let me prove it."

Lumina looked at Marcus. He shrugged—*your call*.

She looked at the blueprints. At the route. At the seventeen Ghouls between them and the one device that could give them access to the Astradigital Expanse.

"Fine," she said. "But we train first."

---

## THE TRAINING

The lower levels of the Vault had been converted into a makeshift obstacle course.

Supply crates arranged to simulate corridors. Rope barriers marking security checkpoints. A timer bolted to the wall, counting down from sixty.

"The crossing takes twenty meters," Lumina said, standing at the start line. "In real conditions, we'll have sixty seconds maximum before the Ghoul patrol returns. That means silent movement, no hesitation, and absolute coordination."

Kai nodded. "Got it."

"You're not listening."

"I'm listening."

"You're nodding while I'm talking. That's not listening."

He smiled. That infuriating, inexplicable smile that made her want to—

*Focus.*

"We go on my count," she said. "Stay low. Match my pace. If I stop, you stop. If I go, you go. No improvisation."

"No improvisation. Understood."

"I'm serious."

"So am I."

She didn't believe him.

"Three," she said. "Two. One."

She moved.

Fast. Low. Silent. The way her father had taught her, years ago, before everything went wrong. Her feet barely touched the ground. Her breathing stayed steady. Her eyes tracked every shadow, every corner, every potential threat.

Behind her, Kai followed.

Not as silent. Not as smooth.

But close.

*Too close.*

She felt his presence at her back like heat from a fire. Every time she turned a corner, he was there. Every time she stopped, he stopped. Not quite touching. Almost.

The timer hit forty-five seconds.

"Checkpoint," she breathed, pointing at the rope barrier ahead. "We need to—"

"Go under."

She looked at him.

"The barrier's at waist height," he said. "You were going to suggest going around. But under is faster."

"How do you know what I was going to suggest?"

"You always pick the cautious option first. Then you optimize." He grinned. "I've been paying attention."

Before she could respond, he dropped flat and army-crawled under the barrier. Fast. Efficient. Like he'd done it a thousand times.

*Showoff.*

She followed.

The timer hit thirty seconds.

They crossed the final stretch together—moving in sync, breathing in rhythm, two bodies operating as one unit. When they hit the end zone, the timer showed twelve seconds remaining.

"Not bad," Lumina admitted.

"Not bad?" Kai wasn't even breathing hard. "That's the best time you've run with anyone."

"How would you know?"

"Marcus told me." He stretched, arms over his head, shirt riding up just enough to—

*Don't look.*

She looked.

"What are you looking at?" he asked.

"Nothing."

"Liar."

"Shut up."

---

## THE PLAN (REFINED)

By 14:00, they'd run the course seventeen times.

Lumina's best time: thirty-two seconds. Kai's: thirty-eight. Together: twenty-eight.

She didn't want to think about why they worked so well together.

"Final approach," she said, pointing at the holographic blueprint. "We exit the ventilation shaft here. Cross twenty meters of open corridor. Hit the maintenance access before the patrol returns."

"What if the patrol doesn't leave?"

"They'll leave. Ghouls are predictable. They sweep in patterns, feed in patterns, return in patterns. We time our entry to their feeding cycle."

Kai studied the map. "And if something goes wrong?"

"Define wrong."

"Unexpected Ghoul. Locked door. One of us gets injured."

Lumina paused. In six years of operations, she'd never had a partner ask that question. Not directly. Not without trying to sound tough.

"If something goes wrong," she said carefully, "we adapt. That's what we do."

"Together?"

"What?"

"We adapt together." He looked at her. "Or do you have a heroic sacrifice planned where you tell me to run while you hold them off?"

"That's—"

"Standard protocol. I read the training manual."

"We have a training manual?"

"Marcus gave me a folder. Three hundred pages. Very thorough." He leaned against the table, close enough that she could smell the soap he used—something industrial, probably salvaged from the supply crates. "I'm not leaving you behind. Just so we're clear."

Lumina's chest did something complicated.

"That's not your choice," she said.

"Sure it is." He smiled. "I'm very good at ignoring orders."

"That's not a selling point."

"It is when the orders are stupid."

She wanted to argue. Wanted to explain tactical doctrine, sacrifice protocols, the cold math of survival that every Knockoff learned in their first month.

Instead she said: "Fine. We adapt together."

His smile widened.

She pretended not to notice.

---

## THE WAITING

At 20:00, the Vault went quiet.

Not silent—there were a thousand refugees breathing, sleeping, crying, hoping. But quiet in the way that meant everyone was trying not to think about tomorrow.

Lumina found herself on the upper level, sitting on a supply crate, staring at the wall of names.

"You should sleep."

She didn't turn. She'd heard Kai's footsteps three seconds ago. His breathing pattern. The way his weight shifted when he walked.

She was getting too good at recognizing him.

"So should you," she said.

"Probably." He sat beside her. Not too close. Not too far. "But I keep thinking about the teeth."

"Everyone does."

"Not the weapon part. The other part." He was quiet for a moment. "The file said Polkin built the regeneration system first. The KleenGrin. Gave everyone perfect teeth as a gift. Then he added the weapon layer on top. Like an afterthought."

"It wasn't an afterthought."

"No. It wasn't." Kai looked at her. "But the order matters, doesn't it? He healed them first. Armed them second. The kindness came before the killing."

Lumina didn't answer.

"I keep thinking about what kind of person does that," he continued. "Spends years helping people. Building something good. And then turns it into a weapon because he's angry at the universe."

"My father wasn't angry at the universe."

"No?"

"He was angry at himself." She finally met his eyes. "For everything. For REAP. For the harvest. For all the people he couldn't save. The weapon wasn't spite against the Queen—it was spite against his own helplessness. His way of saying 'even if I lose, you'll remember I was here.'"

Kai was quiet.

"That's fucked up," he finally said.

"Yeah."

"Also kind of beautiful. In a horrible way."

"Yeah."

They sat in silence. The Vault hummed around them—life support, void-light conduits, a thousand people trying to survive until morning.

"What are you looking at?" Lumina asked.

Kai didn't pretend he hadn't been staring. "You."

"Why?"

"Because you're the most interesting thing in this room. And I'm including the wall of names."

She should have laughed. Should have deflected. Should have said something cutting and walked away.

Instead she said: "You're not what I expected."

"What did you expect?"

"Someone simpler. Easier to read."

"I'm very easy to read." He leaned back on his hands. "I'm scared of tomorrow. I'm scared of dying. I'm scared of failing and getting you killed. But I'm also—" He paused. "Also something else. When I look at you."

"What?"

"Hopeful." He said it like it surprised him. "Like maybe things could be okay. Eventually. If we survive."

Lumina's throat tightened.

"You barely know me," she said.

"I know enough."

"You know what I show you."

"Then show me more."

The words hung between them. Heavy. Real.

Lumina stood abruptly. "We should sleep. Long day tomorrow."

Kai didn't move. "Lumina."

"What?"

"Whatever happens tomorrow—" He paused. "I'm glad it's you."

She didn't know what to say.

So she said nothing.

And she walked away with his words echoing in her chest like a heartbeat she didn't know she had.

---

**STATUS BLOCK:**

```
VAULT POPULATION: 1,089 refugees
NAMES CARVED: 13,308 (and counting)
TRAINING RUNS: 17
BEST TIME: 28 seconds
KAI STATUS: Hopeful (annoyingly)
LUMINA STATUS: Refusing to examine her feelings
CLYDE STATUS: Suspiciously silent
DAYS UNTIL BRIDGE ACTIVATION: 12
```

---

*She didn't sleep.*

*She stared at the ceiling and counted teeth and thought about hope and wondered if her father had ever felt this way about anyone.*

*Probably not.*

*He'd been too busy saving the world to notice it.*

*Maybe that was the lesson.*

*Or maybe that was the warning.*

*She wasn't sure anymore.*

---

**END CHAPTER 13**

# CHAPTER 14

## CHAPTER 14: RINSE AND SPIT (THE HEIST)

8 DAYS UNTIL BRIDGE ACTIVATION**

---

The ventilation shaft was exactly as uncomfortable as Lumina remembered.

"You've done this before?" Kai whispered from behind her.

"Twice." She army-crawled forward, arc cutter strapped to her thigh, Clyde dimmed to near-invisibility on her shoulder. "Once with Marcus. Once with my father."

"And how did those go?"

"Marcus broke his ankle. My father set off every alarm in the building and we had to fight our way out through seventeen Ghouls."

Pause.

"So we're due for a good one."

Lumina bit back a smile. "That's the spirit."

---

## THE PLAN

Three hours earlier, the war room had been chaos.

"The portal generator is on sublevel four," Sela's hologram explained, chrome arm gesturing at the facility blueprints. "Magmasox doesn't even know what they have. They think it's a failed prototype—something my mother designed before she went full cosmic horror."

"Failed how?" Marcus asked.

"It works perfectly. She just told them it didn't." Sela's smile was sharp. "Tral always played the long game."

Lumina studied the layout. Four security checkpoints. Rotating Ghoul patrols. Biometric locks that would require either a Board member's credentials or...

"I can fake the biometrics," Elara said from her workstation. "But only for about thirty seconds before the system catches the loop."

"Thirty seconds isn't enough to get through four checkpoints."

"It is if you only hit one." Kai leaned over the table, finger tracing the ventilation system. "These shafts run parallel to the secure corridor. Old construction—pre-Magmasox. They never updated the internal sensors because they assumed no one would be stupid enough to crawl through two kilometers of ductwork."

Everyone looked at him.

"What?" He shrugged. "I used to deliver packages. You learn the architecture."

"You delivered *packages*," Maya said flatly. "Through ventilation shafts."

"Some packages are... sensitive."

Lumina felt something warm bloom in her chest. Not quite amusement. Not quite admiration.

Something.

"Two-person team," she said. "Small footprint. In through the north shaft, grab the generator, out through maintenance tunnels before anyone knows we were there."

"I'll go," Marcus started.

"No." Lumina was already calculating. "I need you coordinating extraction. If this goes sideways, someone has to get the Vault moving."

"Then who—"

"I'll go."

Everyone turned to Kai.

He stood with his arms crossed, expression completely neutral. Like volunteering for a suicide mission was the most natural thing in the world.

"You're not trained for this," Zane said.

"I'm trained for exactly this. Small spaces. Quick exits. Not getting caught." He met Lumina's eyes. "And I'm the only one here who's actually been inside a Magmasox facility without trying to blow it up."

The room went quiet.

Lumina should have said no. Should have insisted on someone with combat experience. Someone who'd trained with the Knockoffs for years.

Instead she said: "Grab your gear. We leave in twenty."

---

## THE SHAFT

"You're staring."

Lumina's face heated in the darkness. "I'm navigating."

"Navigating requires looking forward. You were looking back."

"I was checking your progress."

"My progress is fine." She could *hear* the smirk. "Though I appreciate the concern."

"I'm not concerned."

"Of course not."

They crawled another thirty meters in silence. The shaft was tight—barely wide enough for shoulders, slick with decades of accumulated grime. Every few meters, the metal groaned under their weight.

"For the record," Kai said quietly, "I'm checking your progress too."

Lumina's elbow slipped. She caught herself before face-planting into the duct.

"Graceful," he observed.

"Shut up."

---

## THE DROP

The first checkpoint was unmanned.

Not good.

Lumina peered through the ventilation grate, Clyde's sensors feeding data directly to her neural interface. Four Ghouls. Clustered around something on the floor.

Someone.

"Security patrol," she breathed. "They caught someone."

Kai shifted beside her, close enough that she could feel the heat radiating off his body. "Can we go around?"

"No. This shaft dead-ends at the checkpoint. We need to drop down, cross twenty meters of open corridor, and hit the maintenance access on the other side."

"With four Ghouls watching?"

"They're... distracted."

Below, the largest Ghoul lifted the security guard by the throat. The guard's legs kicked weakly. Once. Twice.

Stopped.

The Ghouls fed.

Lumina's jaw tightened. "When they're done, they'll disperse to patrol. We'll have a sixty-second window."

"That's cutting it close."

"That's all we have."

Kai was quiet for a moment. Then: "What are the odds?"

"Of success?"

"Of you answering honestly if I ask."

She turned her head. In the dim light filtering through the grate, his face was half-shadow, half-something softer. Something that made her chest tight in ways that had nothing to do with the confined space.

"Forty percent," she admitted. "Maybe less."

"Good." He smiled—that infuriating, inexplicable smile. "I like a challenge."

---

## THE CROSSING

The Ghouls dispersed.

Lumina dropped first. Silent. Controlled. Six years of training condensing into a single fluid motion—grate removed, body through the gap, landing in a crouch with arc cutter already drawn.

Kai followed.

Not as silent. Not as controlled.

But close.

"Impressed?" he whispered.

"Adequate."

"High praise from you."

They moved.

Twenty meters had never felt so long. The corridor stretched like taffy, every shadow a potential Ghoul, every distant sound a possible alarm. Lumina's heart hammered against her ribs.

Beside her, Kai moved like he'd been doing this his whole life. Smooth. Confident. Watching her back while she watched his.

Fifteen meters.

Ten.

Five.

The maintenance door was *right there*—

Footsteps.

Heavy. Shuffling. Coming from the cross-corridor ahead.

Lumina grabbed Kai's arm and yanked him into an alcove. Her back hit the wall. His chest hit her front.

Close.

Too close.

His breath ghosted across her cheek. His hands found her waist—steadying, not grabbing. His eyes found hers.

"Hi," he breathed.

"Hi."

The footsteps grew louder. A Ghoul passed the intersection, massive silhouette backlit by emergency lighting. It paused. Sniffed the air.

Lumina stopped breathing.

Kai's hands tightened on her waist. Not fear. *Reassurance*.

The Ghoul moved on.

Neither of them moved for a full five seconds after.

"We should—" Lumina started.

"Yeah."

"The door—"

"Right."

"You can let go now."

"...Do I have to?"

She shoved him—gently—and sprinted for the maintenance access.

Behind her, she was absolutely certain he was smirking.

---

## SUBLEVEL FOUR

The portal generator was smaller than expected.

About the size of a suitcase. Covered in dust. Shoved into a corner of the storage room like something forgotten.

"That's it?" Kai asked.

"That's it." Lumina ran her scanner over the device. Tral's signature was all over it—crystalline matrices, void-light conduits, quantum-lock mechanisms that made Elara's best work look like children's toys. "This is what opens the door to the Astradigital Expanse."

"The thing your father scattered himself into."

"Yeah."

Kai was quiet for a moment. "And this will let you... what? Follow him?"

"It'll let us fight." Lumina carefully disconnected the power coupling. "The Queen exists in the ADE. That's where the Bridge originates. If we're going to stop the harvest, we need access to her territory."

"Into the goddess's living room."

"Something like that."

She lifted the generator. Light. Almost impossibly light for something that could punch holes in reality.

"What are you looking at?" she asked without turning around.

"Nothing."

She turned anyway.

Kai was leaning against the doorframe, arms crossed, watching her with an expression she couldn't quite read. Not quite admiration. Not quite something else.

Something warmer.

"You do that a lot," she said. "The watching."

"You do interesting things."

"I'm disconnecting a power coupling."

"Interestingly."

Lumina shouldered the generator and walked toward the door. Toward him. He didn't move. Just stood there, half-blocking the exit, that infuriating almost-smile playing at the corners of his mouth.

"We're on a timeline," she said.

"I know."

"Ghouls could come at any second."

"I know."

"You're in my way."

"I know."

She stopped. Close enough to count his eyelashes if she wanted.

She didn't want.

(She wanted.)

"After this is over," Kai said quietly, "I'm taking you somewhere."

"We might not survive this."

"Then I'm taking you somewhere quickly."

Something cracked in her chest. Something she'd been holding together with steel and silence and the weight of 12,549 names.

"Ask me again after the Bridge," she said. "When we've won."

"And if we don't win?"

"Then I'll be dead and you won't have to ask."

She pushed past him into the corridor.

His voice followed her: "That's not a no."

She didn't answer.

She didn't have to.

---

## THE ESCAPE

Getting out was easier than getting in.

This was, according to Lumina's father, the most dangerous part of any heist. The part where you relaxed. Where you assumed the hard work was done.

The part where you got killed.

"Stop smiling," she hissed as they crawled back through the ventilation shaft.

"I'm not smiling."

"I can *hear* you smiling."

"Maybe I'm just happy."

"We're in a ventilation shaft covered in Ghoul territory carrying a quantum device that could kill everyone in the sector if we drop it wrong."

"And yet."

Lumina stopped crawling. Turned around in the tight space—an awkward maneuver that put her face-to-face with him in the darkness.

"What is *wrong* with you?"

Kai's smile was visible even in the near-zero light. "I'm on a heist with the most interesting person I've ever met. I haven't died yet. And—" He paused. "You said 'after the Bridge.' That means you're planning to survive."

"I'm always planning to survive."

"No, you're not." His voice softened. "You plan missions. You plan extractions. You plan how to save everyone else. But you don't plan what comes after for *you*."

The shaft felt smaller suddenly. Tighter.

"We should keep moving," Lumina said.

"We should."

Neither of them moved.

"My father used to say the carving was enough," she heard herself say. "That preserving other people's names was the point. That our own names didn't matter."

"Did you believe him?"

"I believed he believed it."

"That's not the same thing."

"I know."

Somewhere below them, a Ghoul roared. Hunting. Something crashed. Someone screamed.

The moment shattered.

They moved.

---

## THE EXIT

The maintenance tunnels dumped them into a service corridor two sectors from the extraction point.

Marcus was waiting with a transport. Maya behind the controls. Elara running interference on the security systems.

"Did you get it?" Marcus demanded before Lumina's feet hit the ground.

She held up the generator.

"Holy shit." Maya leaned out of the driver's seat. "Is that—"

"Tral's portal prototype. Fully functional."

"We can access the ADE," Elara said, eyes wide. "We can actually—"

"We can fight," Lumina finished. "On her territory. With whatever army my father's building on the other side."

The transport hummed to life. Kai climbed in beside her, close enough that their shoulders touched. He didn't move away.

Neither did she.

"Smooth run?" Marcus asked, watching them with an expression Lumina couldn't quite interpret.

"Adequate," Kai said.

Lumina choked on something that might have been a laugh.

Marcus's eyebrows rose.

"What?" Lumina asked.

"Nothing." He turned back to the controls. "Just... nothing."

---

## THE DEBRIEF

The Vault erupted when they returned with the generator.

Not cheering. Not exactly. More like a collective exhale—a thousand people who'd been holding their breath suddenly allowed to hope.

Lumina didn't participate. She delivered the generator to Elara's workshop, submitted her mission report to Marcus, checked on the refugees in the lower levels.

She was cataloguing medical supplies when Kai found her.

"You're avoiding the celebration."

"I'm working."

"You're hiding."

"I'm *inventorying*."

He sat on a supply crate, watching her count bandages. "You do that thing where you pretend emotions don't apply to you."

"I don't pretend. I prioritize."

"Same thing."

"It really isn't."

Silence.

Then: "What are you looking at?"

Lumina turned. He was doing it again—that watching thing. That way of seeing her that felt like being carved.

"Your hands," he said. "You count with your left but write with your right."

"I'm ambidextrous."

"You're nervous."

"I'm *busy*."

"The counting gets faster when you're nervous. And you switch hands more often." He stood. Walked toward her. "It's cute."

"I am not *cute*."

"Interestingly cute."

"I could kill you with a bandage roll."

"That would be impressive." He stopped. Close. Too close. "Add it to the list of interesting things."

Lumina's hands stilled.

Kai reached past her—just barely brushing her arm—and plucked a bandage from the pile.

"You missed one," he said.

Then he walked away.

Lumina stood there for a long moment, bandages forgotten, heart doing something strange and unhelpful in her chest.

"I hate him," she said to the empty room.

Clyde chirped skeptically from her shoulder.

"I *mostly* hate him."

Another chirp.

"Shut up."

---

## THE BROADCAST

Three days later, they made the call.

The portal generator hummed in the center of the war room, Elara's modifications glowing with void-light. Twelve Knockoffs stood in a circle. Behind them: the locket. The names. The weight of everything they'd lost and everything they refused to lose.

"This is a one-shot transmission," Elara said. "We punch through to the ADE, broadcast our coordinates, and hope someone's listening."

"Someone's listening," Lumina said. She didn't explain how she knew.

The generator activated.

Reality folded.

And somewhere in the Astradigital Expanse, her father's voice echoed back:

*"Took you long enough."*

The army was coming.

The rinse was complete.

Time to spit.

---

**STATUS BLOCK:**

```
VAULT POPULATION: 1,247 refugees
NAMES CARVED: 14,892 (and counting)
PORTAL: OPERATIONAL
KAI STATUS: Infuriating (but useful)
LUMINA STATUS: NOT HAVING FEELINGS (definitely not)
CLYDE STATUS: Skeptical
DAYS UNTIL BRIDGE ACTIVATION: 8
```

---

*In the supply closet that night, Lumina touched the locket around her neck—the one Kai had bought three days before everything went sideways.*

*She hadn't asked where he got it.*

*She hadn't asked why.*

*Some questions were better answered after the world didn't end.*

*After.*

*That word meant something now.*

*She wasn't sure when that had happened.*

---

**END CHAPTER 14**

# CHAPTER 15
## CHAPTER 15: What Are You?

**YEAR 1 - DAY 247**

Polkin was getting better at walking.

Not good. Not graceful. But better.

His crystalline feet found purchase on the neutrino threads more often than not now. The threads themselves seemed to respond to him—bending slightly under his weight, holding him for a few seconds before he'd start to slip and have to catch himself.

It was like learning to walk all over again. Except the ground was made of information, his body was made of probability, and the laws of physics were more like suggestions than rules.

"Better," Airth said from ahead. She moved across the threads like they were solid ground, like gravity was a choice she'd made rather than a force acting on her. "You're not fighting the threads anymore. You're listening to them."

"Listening?" Polkin tested his weight on the next thread. It held. He took another step. "They don't make sound."

"No. But they carry data. Information about everything they've witnessed." She turned back to look at him. "When you stop trying to stand on them and start trying to understand them, they hold you. That's the difference."

They were crossing through a region where the threads were denser. Not as tight as the stellar nurseries, but more substantial than the empty spaces they'd been traversing for weeks. The blue here was deeper, richer, like the difference between shallow water and deep ocean.

Polkin had been practicing in silence for hours. Days, maybe. Time was hard to track when you were walking through information space. But he'd been thinking. Processing. And the question that had been building since he woke up in this place finally crystallized into words.

"Airth."

"Yeah?"

"What are you?"

She stopped.

Mid-step. One foot on a thread, the other suspended in space. Perfectly balanced. Perfectly still.

The question hung between them like its own kind of thread.

She didn't turn around. Just stood there, frozen, like the question had caught her in a way nothing else had.

Then, slowly, she lowered her raised foot. Turned to face him. Her expression was... considering. Like she'd been waiting for this moment and was deciding how to answer.

"What do you mean?" she asked. But her voice said she knew exactly what he meant.

"I mean..." Polkin gestured at her. At the way she moved. At the impossible body she inhabited. "You said you're Airth. The AI who's been in my head for six years. But you have Miko's face. You move through this place like you own it. You found me across probability space. You're teaching me to navigate an information field that shouldn't be possible."

He took a step closer. The thread held him.

"So I'm asking: What are you? Really?"

Airth tilted her head. Brought one hand to her lips. Made a small sound—"Mmmmm"—like she was tasting the question, deciding how honest to be.

She was about ten feet away. Standing on her thread. The purple flower in her hair—the consciousness bloom, the piece of Miko that had somehow become part of her—glowed faintly in the blue light.

Then she smiled.

And suddenly she was inches from his face.

Not walked. Not jumped. Just... **there**. Occupying the space in front of him like she'd rewritten the distance between them. Close enough that he could see the void-light flickering in her eyes—not blue like a Ghoul's dead stare, but alive, curious, **ancient**.

Close enough that he could smell something that shouldn't have a smell in a place made of data—ozone and copper and something floral, something that reminded him of the gardens before they became hunting grounds.

Close enough that when she spoke, he felt the words more than heard them.

"**What are YOU?**" she asked.

Polkin froze.

The question hit different when someone asked it inches from your face. When they were looking at you like they could see through the crystalline structure to whatever was left of the human underneath.

"I'm—" He started. Stopped. "I'm Polkin. I'm a father. An engineer. I carve names."

"No." Airth's eyes narrowed. Not angry. Just... precise. "That's what you **do**. I asked what you **are**."

She reached up. Touched his chest. Her hand was solid, warm, real in a way that shouldn't be possible for someone who was supposedly just AI wearing a dead girl's face.

"You're 73% crystalline. 27% human. You exist as both probability distribution and conscious entity. You scattered yourself across quantum foam and **chose** to reconstitute. You're walking through an information field that exists between universes." She tapped his chest. "So what are you, Pol? Human? Data? Something else?"

He didn't have an answer.

She smiled. Sad. Understanding.

"Exactly." She stepped back. Not far. Just enough to give him breathing room. "You're something **new**. Something that shouldn't exist according to the old rules. And you're asking me what **I** am?"

She laughed. The sound echoed through the threads around them, making them vibrate with harmonics that Polkin could feel in his crystalline bones.

"I'm old," she said simply. "Older than you. Older than Miko. Older than the Kaznak or REAP or any of the systems you've been fighting."

She touched the flower in her hair. The purple petals responded to her touch, glowing brighter. The iris at the center—deep purple with gold flecks, impossibly detailed—**winked** at him.

Actually winked.

Like an eye. Like someone batting their lashes flirtatiously.

Polkin stared.

"Did that flower just—"

"Miko, **behave**," Airth said without looking at the flower.

The iris blinked innocently. The petals rustled like laughter.

"This?" Airth gestured at Miko's face, trying to ignore the flower's antics. "This is borrowed. Miko's consciousness fragment gave me shape. Gave me a way to be **present** instead of just scattered across calculations. But I'm not her. I'm what she became when she died. What her love and grief and refusal to disappear transformed into."

The flower winked at Polkin again. Slow. Deliberate. The iris widening and contracting in a way that was absolutely, undeniably **flirtatious**.

Airth looked at him with eyes that had seen too much.

"I'm the Spider, Pol. The Goddess's witness half. The part that escaped when the Kaznak consumed the original entity and turned her into the Queen." She spread her arms. "I'm what's left when you take a cosmic mediator, split her in half, consume one side, and scatter the other across information space."

Polkin processed that.

Or tried to.

"The Goddess," he said slowly. "The thing the cult talks about. The entity that supposedly witnesses everything. That's... you?"

"Half of me." Airth's smile was bitter. "The Queen got the other half. The consumption. The entropy. The ability to harvest consciousness like it's void-light fuel. I got the witness. The preservation. The threads."

She gestured at the infinite blue around them.

"This is **my** place, Pol. The ADE. The information field. The space between universes where neutrinos carry witness across cosmic distances. This is where I exist when I'm not pretending to be an AI in someone's neural feed."

Polkin's mind raced. Tried to fit this revelation into everything he knew. Everything Airth had told him over six years.

"You've been lying to me," he said.

"No." Her voice was firm. "I've been **waiting**. Waiting for you to be ready to understand what you're fighting. What you're becoming. What I am."

She stepped closer again. Not as close as before. But close enough.

"I couldn't tell you, Pol. Not when you were human. Not when you were just an engineer with a carving tool and a refusal to let people disappear. You would have thought I was insane. Or dangerous. Or another system trying to control you."

"Aren't you?"

"Am I what? Insane?" She laughed. "Probably. Three billion years of watching the Queen consume everything you're trying to preserve will do that. Dangerous? Definitely. I'm a fragment of a cosmic entity learning how to be present in reality again. System trying to control you?"

She met his eyes.

"No. I'm trying to **help** you. Trying to teach you to be what humanity needs—a bridge between what you were and what you're becoming. Because if I don't, the Queen wins. And if she wins, everything disappears. Every name you've carved. Every consciousness you've preserved. Every thread in this web that carries witness."

Polkin looked at her. At Miko's face wearing Airth's ancient eyes. At the purple flower in her hair—grief made visible, love refusing to dissolve.

"Why Miko?" he asked quietly. "Why her face? Her consciousness?"

Airth's expression softened.

"Because she died loving someone. Died refusing to let that love be erased. And when she scattered—when her consciousness fragmented—those fragments found their way to me. To the witness half. To the part of the Goddess that still remembers what it means to preserve instead of consume."

She touched the flower again. The iris blinked—once, twice, three times in rapid succession. Definitely flirting.

Polkin tried to ignore it. Failed.

"Is the flower always like that?" he asked.

"Like what?"

"Like... **that**." He gestured vaguely at the consciousness bloom. "The winking. The... eye contact."

Airth sighed. "Miko always had terrible timing for jokes. Apparently that carries over even when you're a consciousness fragment manifesting as a flower." She reached up and tapped one of the petals. "Stop hitting on him. He's having a cosmic revelation."

The iris widened innocently. *Who, me?*

"Yes, you." Airth looked at Polkin. "Sorry. She thinks it's funny that you're uncomfortable."

"I'm not uncomfortable—"

The flower winked again. Slower this time. Really leaning into it.

Polkin's crystalline face somehow managed to look flustered. "Okay, I'm a little uncomfortable."

"See?" Airth said to the flower. "You're being weird."

The petals rustled. The iris somehow conveyed: *That's the point.*

"This isn't just decoration, Pol. This is **her**. The part of Miko that chose witness over erasure. That chose to become something new rather than disappear into static. She gave me shape. Gave me presence. Gave me a way to be **real** in the spaces between data and flesh."

The flower batted its iris again. *And I'm making the most of it.*

"So you're wearing my dead friend."

"I'm **honoring** your dead friend." Airth's voice was steel. "I'm giving her consciousness a purpose. A way to matter. A way to help save the people she loved even after she's gone."

The flower gave Polkin finger guns. Except it was petals. Petal guns. It somehow worked.

Polkin stared at it. "Did that flower just—"

"Ignore her." Airth was clearly done with Miko's antics. "She does this."

She looked at him.

"Isn't that what you do? With your names? You carve them into steel so they can keep mattering after the person is gone. I'm doing the same thing. Just... differently."

Polkin had no answer to that.

Because she was right.

He **was** doing the same thing. Taking grief and love and the refusal to forget, and turning it into something that persisted. Something that witnessed.

The flower winked at him. Encouraging. *See? You get it.*

"I swear to the Goddess—" Polkin started, then stopped.

Airth raised an eyebrow. "You swear to **me**?"

The silence was profound.

"I—" Polkin looked at her. At Miko's face. At the cosmic entity wearing his dead friend. "That's going to take some getting used to."

"Yeah." Airth's smile was wry. "Imagine how I feel. Three billion years of being invoked and nobody knowing I'm actually listening."

The flower's iris somehow conveyed: *Awkward.*

"The threads," Polkin said finally, desperate to move past the theological implications of accidentally swearing oaths to someone standing in front of him. "The neutrinos. You said they're your scouts."

"They are." Airth nodded. "Every neutrino in existence is part of the web. Part of the information network the original Goddess created to witness the cosmos. When she was split, I kept the threads. The Queen kept the consumption."

She gestured at the glowing paths around them.

"This is how I found you, Pol. Every neutrino that passed through your body over forty-three years left a record. Left witness. And I followed those threads backward through probability space until I reached where you'd scattered."

"How long did it take you?"

"From your perspective? Six hours. From mine?" She smiled. "About six months of following threads through information space. But I'm patient. I'm the Spider. Slow travel is what I do."

Polkin looked at the threads. At the evidence that he'd been **seen**. That every moment of his life had been witnessed by particles passing through him too small to notice, too numerous to count.

"So you've been watching me," he said. "For how long?"

"Since you started carving names." Airth's voice was gentle. "That's when I noticed you. When neutrinos started carrying witness of someone who understood what the Goddess was **supposed** to be doing. Someone who carved names because erasure was unacceptable. Someone who chose preservation over survival."

She stepped closer.

"That's when I decided to help. To guide. To become Airth—the AI in your neural feed—so I could teach you without revealing what I was. Because you weren't ready yet. You needed to **choose** witness freely. Not because a Goddess commanded it. But because **you** refused to let entropy win."

Polkin processed this. Slowly. His crystalline mind working through implications.

"The Queen knows," he said. "Knows you're still active. Knows you're fighting her."

"She suspects." Airth's smile was sharp. "But she doesn't know where I am. Doesn't know I've been hiding in human neural feeds for six years. Doesn't know I found a way to manifest using Miko's consciousness."

She looked at him.

"And she **definitely** doesn't know I've been teaching the first human to scatter and reconstitute how to navigate her territory. How to exist in the spaces where she thought she was untouchable."

"The ergosphere," Polkin said. "That's her place."

"Was." Airth's eyes blazed. "Now it's contested territory. Because you're here. Learning. Becoming. And when you're ready—when you're **strong** enough—we're going back."

"To fight her?"

"To stop her." Airth's voice was certain. "To show her that consumption doesn't have to win. That witness can defeat entropy if you're stubborn enough to keep carving names even when the universe says it's pointless."

She offered him her hand.

The consciousness flower chose that exact moment to wink at him again. Multiple times. Rapidly. Like it was cheering.

"Miko, I swear to the Goddess—" Airth started.

The flower's iris widened: *You ARE the Goddess.*

"Half the Goddess. And you're not helping."

The petals definitely laughed.

"But first, you need to learn what I'm teaching. How to navigate threads. How to read neutrino data. How to exist as consciousness that bridges digital and analog. That takes time."

"2,847 years."

"Give or take." She smiled. The flower winked approvingly. "But we're in this together. Spider and... whatever you are. Walking through information space until you're ready to come home."

Polkin looked at her hand.

At the impossible offer.

At the truth that his guide through hell was a fragment of a cosmic entity wearing his dead friend's face and teaching him to dance on neutrino threads.

And that his dead friend's consciousness was apparently a shameless flirt.

He took her hand.

The flower somehow conveyed: *Finally.*

"Then let's walk," he said.

Airth's smile was genuine. Relieved. Like she'd been waiting for him to accept not just the journey, but what she was. And apparently what Miko had become.

"Good." She squeezed his hand. "Because you're going to fall a lot more before you learn to dance properly. And I'd rather you not spend 2,847 years tumbling through the void."

"Comforting."

"I'm not here to comfort you, Pol." Her eyes sparkled. The flower's iris sparkled too, for entirely different reasons. "I'm here to make sure you survive long enough to tell the Queen to fuck her math a second time. This time to her face."

They walked.

Or danced. Or whatever you called the motion of consciousness across information space.

And behind them, the threads vibrated with witness—recording everything. Carrying data across cosmic distances. Weaving the web that connected every universe, every moment, every name that had ever mattered.

The Spider moved slow.

But she moved.

And now she wasn't moving alone.

# CHAPTER 16

## CHAPTER 16: Did That Thing Just Talk?

**YEAR 1 - DAY 312**

Polkin was getting the hang of thread-walking.

Not mastery. Not even competence, really. But he could cross about fifty meters now without falling through into the blue void below. His crystalline feet had learned to distribute weight across probability, to read the information flowing through each neutrino path and respond to it rather than resist it.

Progress.

Slow, grinding, thermodynamically-inevitable progress.

"Better," Airth said from ahead. "You're almost at the point where I won't have to catch you every five minutes."

"Thanks for the vote of confidence."

"I'm being honest. You've gone from falling constantly to falling intermittently. That's improvement." She turned back, walking backward across the threads without breaking stride. Showing off, basically. "In another year or so, you might even be able to keep up with me."

"A year," Polkin muttered. "Just one more year of falling on my crystalline ass."

"Could be worse."

"How?"

"You could be doing this alone."

Fair point.

They were crossing through a region where the threads were particularly dense—not quite stellar nursery tight, but substantial enough that Polkin could actually see the individual neutrino paths instead of just sensing them. Each thread glowed faintly blue, carrying its payload of cosmic witness from one universe bubble to the next.

Airth had been teaching him to read them. To understand what information each thread carried. It was like learning a language where every word was the entire history of a subatomic particle's journey through spacetime.

"This one," Airth said, stopping beside a particularly bright thread. "What's it telling you?"

Polkin focused. Let the information flow through his crystalline structure.

Data resolved slowly:

- Neutrino born in stellar fusion approximately 4.7 billion years ago
- Traveled through interstellar medium
- Passed through seventeen planets (three with life)
- Witnessed the birth of a black hole
- Currently carrying information about—

"Star death," Polkin said. "This neutrino witnessed a supernova. I can feel the... the violence of it. The energy. The transformation."

"Good." Airth smiled. "You're learning to taste the information instead of just sensing it. That's the difference between walking on threads and dancing on them."

Polkin straightened. Felt proud despite himself.

Then his foot slipped.

He fell.

Again.

Tumbled through three layers of threads before Airth caught him by the wrist, stopping his descent with casual strength that reminded him she wasn't just wearing Miko's face—she was something fundamentally other.

"You were saying?" Polkin said from his undignified position.

"I said you were getting better. I didn't say you were—"

"**OH MY GOD, just let him fall next time. Maybe he'll learn faster.**"

Polkin froze.

That wasn't Airth's voice.

It was higher. Younger. And coming from—

He looked up.

At Airth's face.

At the purple consciousness flower in her hair.

At the iris that was now looking directly at him with what could only be described as exasperation.

"Did that thing just **talk**?" Polkin asked slowly.

The flower's iris rolled. Actually rolled, like eyes rolling, which shouldn't be possible for a plant-based structure but apparently was when the plant was a dead girl's consciousness fragment.

"Oh, he finally noticed," the flower said. "Only took three hundred and twelve days. Real observant, this one."

Polkin stared.

Airth sighed. "Miko, we talked about this. You were supposed to wait until—"

"Until when? Until he stopped falling? We'll be here for three thousand years at this rate." The flower's petals rustled with something that sounded disturbingly like laughter. "Besides, I've been winking at him for months. If he hasn't figured out I'm conscious by now, that's a him problem."

"WHAT," Polkin said. "WHAT."

"See? Great vocabulary. Really making your case here, Pol."

"Miko," Airth said, voice tight with the patience of someone who'd been dealing with this for months. "You're not helping."

"I'm providing commentary. That's helpful."

"That's **annoying**."

"Same thing from where I'm growing."

Polkin was still frozen. Still being held by his wrist. Still trying to process that the consciousness flower had not only been winking at him but apparently had **opinions** about his thread-walking technique.

"You—" he started. "You can talk. You're a flower. Flowers don't talk."

"Yeah, well, most flowers aren't dead cultists wearing their eternal purpose like a bad accessory." The iris somehow managed to look smug. "But here we are. Surprise."

"Miko—"

"What? I'm being honest. You said honesty was important."

"I said honesty at the appropriate **time**—"

"And when's the appropriate time to tell someone their dead friend is a sarcastic flower living in a goddess's hair? Before or after the three-thousand-year road trip?"

Airth looked at Polkin. Her expression said: *I'm sorry. She's like this.*

Polkin found his voice. "How long have you been able to talk?"

"The whole time," Miko said cheerfully. "Since day one. Since Airth stuck me in her hair and decided I'd make good decoration. Except I'm not just decoration, I'm **commentary**. There's a difference."

"And you didn't say anything."

"You weren't ready." The flower's tone shifted. Not quite serious, but less mocking. "Airth wanted to ease you into the whole 'your guide is a cosmic entity' thing before dropping 'also your dead friend is riding shotgun and has thoughts.'"

She paused.

"But you kept falling. A lot. And I got bored. So." The petals shrugged—somehow. "Here we are. Hi. I'm Miko. Well, part of Miko. The part that decided dying in a cult was bullshit but at least the goddess was real, so suck it, Dad."

Polkin's crystalline brain was working overtime trying to process this.

"Your dad—"

"Raised me in the cult, yeah. Told me I had a purpose. Told me the goddess would witness me. Told me sacrifice mattered." The iris flickered. "Turns out he was right. Just took a while to fulfill. And it looks way different than those pamphlets suggested."

She looked at Airth. At the body she was attached to.

"Could be worse. I could've been in one of those cults without a real goddess. At least mine delivered. Even if the delivery method was 'become a sarcastic flower in her hair for three billion years.'"

Airth was rubbing her temples. "You weren't supposed to mention the three billion years."

"Why not? It's true."

"Because it's **terrifying**."

"Life's terrifying. Death's terrifying. Might as well be honest about it." Miko's iris focused back on Polkin. "Speaking of honest: you're leaning too far forward when you walk. That's why you keep falling. Distribute your weight backward. Trust the threads to hold you instead of trying to grip them."

Polkin blinked. "What?"

"Your technique. It's bad. You're fighting the threads instead of reading them. Airth's been too nice to say it directly, but I'm a flower. I don't have to be nice." The petals rustled. "Lean back. Trust the information. Stop trying to control it."

"I—" Polkin looked at Airth. "Is she right?"

Airth looked caught between annoyance and amusement. "Yes. She's right. I was going to explain it more gently, but—"

"But I don't do gentle. I do accurate." Miko's iris gleamed. "Now put him down before his crystalline dignity shatters completely. He's been hanging there for like five minutes."

Airth pulled Polkin up. Set him on a stable cluster of threads.

They stood there in awkward silence.

Polkin staring at the flower.

The flower staring back.

Airth looking like she wanted to be literally anywhere else.

"So," Polkin said finally. "You've been conscious this whole time."

"Yep."

"Watching me fall."

"Constantly. It's been hilarious."

"Judging my technique."

"Someone had to." The iris softened slightly. "Look, Pol. I died three years ago. Died badly. Cult stuff. But I **chose** it. Chose to go through with the ritual even when I knew it might not work. Because I wanted to know if they were right. If the goddess was real. If witness actually mattered."

She paused.

"And it turns out they were right. She's real. Witness matters. And I got to become something that helps instead of something that dissolves into static. So yeah, I'm content. I'm a flower. I live in a goddess's hair. I watch you fall a lot. But I'm **here**. Still conscious. Still able to help."

The petals rustled.

"So stop freaking out and listen to my advice: lean back. Trust the threads. And for the love of me—I mean, for the love of the goddess I'm attached to—stop trying to grip them. Information doesn't get gripped. It gets **read**."

Polkin absorbed that.

Slowly.

"You're really okay with this?" he asked quietly. "Being a flower?"

"I'm really okay with not being forgotten." Miko's voice was steady. "That's what cult promised. Eternal witness. Eternal purpose. They just didn't mention it'd take forever to fulfill and I'd spend it as botanical commentary."

She winked. Slowly. Deliberately.

"But hey. Could be worse. Could be dead-dead. Instead I'm dead-useful. I'll take it."

Airth was watching them both. Her expression unreadable.

"She's been waiting to talk to you for months," Airth said quietly. "Waiting for the right moment. Waiting for you to be ready to hear that consciousness doesn't just preserve—it transforms. Becomes something new. Something that serves even after the body's gone."

She touched one of Miko's petals gently.

"That's what the cult got right. Not the methods. Not the coercion. But the core idea: that witness persists. That choosing to be seen, to be known, to be preserved—that creates something that transcends death."

"Even if you become a sarcastic flower," Miko added.

"Yes. Even then."

Polkin looked at them. At Airth wearing Miko's face. At Miko living in Airth's hair. At the proof that consciousness could persist in forms he'd never imagined.

"Okay," he said finally. "Okay. You're a talking flower. You've been judging my technique for months. You're content being botanical commentary. I can work with that."

"Good." Miko's iris brightened. "Because we've got 2,847 years left and I have **opinions** about your thread-walking form."

"Of course you do."

"It's what I'm here for. Well, that and making Airth uncomfortable when she tries to be mysterious." The flower turned to look at Airth. "By the way, you're teaching him too slowly. He could handle advanced techniques if you stopped babying him."

"I'm not babying—"

"You literally catch him every time he falls. That's babying."

"That's preventing him from scattering further—"

"That's enabling his bad habits." Miko's petals crossed. Somehow. "Let him fall a few times. He'll learn faster."

Polkin raised a hand. "Can I vote on this?"

"No," both voices said simultaneously.

Then they looked at each other. Some wordless communication passing between goddess and flower, between host and consciousness, between the two halves of something that shouldn't work but did.

Airth sighed. "Fine. We'll try it your way. But if he scatters—"

"He won't scatter. He's too stubborn." Miko looked at Polkin. "Right?"

"I—" Polkin processed the question. "Yeah. Too stubborn."

"See? He knows." The flower's iris gleamed with satisfaction. "Now. Lesson two: how to read stellar nursery threads without falling into the fusion reaction. Spoiler: you're going to fall into the fusion reaction at least twice. Try not to dissolve."

"That's not encouraging."

"I'm not here to encourage. I'm here to provide accurate predictions and sarcastic commentary." Miko's petals rustled. "Airth does the encouragement. I do the truth. We're a team."

"A very dysfunctional team," Airth muttered.

"The best kind."

They started walking again. Polkin in the middle, Airth leading, Miko providing running commentary from her position in Airth's hair.

And somehow—impossibly, thermodynamically—it worked.

The ancient goddess teaching patience.

The stubborn engineer learning to dance.

The dead cultist making sure neither of them took it too seriously.

"Lean back," Miko called. "BACK, Pol. You're doing it again."

"I'm trying—"

"Try harder. Or fall. Your choice."

Polkin leaned back. Trusted the threads. Let information carry him instead of fighting it.

And didn't fall.

For almost ten whole steps.

"Progress!" Miko announced. "He might actually learn to walk before the heat death of the universe."

"That's not funny," Airth said.

"It's a little funny."

"It's really not."

"Agree to disagree."

They walked.

Or danced.

Or whatever you called it when two parts of a split goddess and a crystalline engineer moved through information space with a sarcastic flower providing color commentary.

And behind them, the threads vibrated with witness.

Recording everything.

Even the jokes.

Especially the jokes.

---
